Chapter 7: A Certain Debt Collector's Complaint

The morning sun did not reach the back alleys of Kabukicho.

Nagata's day began at 11 a.m., when the neon lights had already faded to gray and the pachinko parlors were just opening their doors. He ate a bowl of convenience store soba standing up, the broth lukewarm, the noodles too soft. His phone lay on the counter beside the disposable chopsticks, the screen dark.

He had debts to collect. Six names on his list. Four were overdue. One was a hostess who worked at a club near the station.

He had been doing this for seven years. He knew the rhythm.

The club was called Lapis, on the second floor of a building that had once been a love hotel. The staircase smelled like bleach and stale cigarettes. The door was unlocked.

He walked in without knocking.

The interior was empty at this hour, the chairs stacked on tables, the disco ball dormant, a vacuum cleaner abandoned in the middle of the floor. A woman in a tracksuit was mopping the bar, her hair tied up in a scarf, her face pale with exhaustion.

She saw him and stopped moving.

"Nagata-san."

"Tomoko." He pulled out a folded paper from his jacket—a loan agreement, stamped and signed, the ink fading. "Your payment is three weeks overdue."

She did not look at the paper. She looked at the mop in her hands, at the floor, at the wall behind him. Anywhere but his face.

"I don't have it."

"You always say that."

"This time I mean it." She gripped the mop handle. "I lost my regulars. The new girl from Roppongi is taking all the tables. I made ¥80,000 last week. Rent is ¥120,000. I have nothing left."

Nagata folded the paper and put it back in his jacket. "Then we have a problem."

"I know." Her voice cracked. "I know. I'm trying. I'm selling my bags. I have a friend who might lend me money. Just give me two more weeks."

"Two weeks." He nodded slowly. "And if you don't have it then?"

She did not answer.

He walked closer, his shoes squeaking on the wet floor. He stopped three feet from her, close enough to see the fear in her eyes.

"I can make this go away," he said. "I can call your boss and say we're extending the term. I can give you three months, with a lower interest rate. I can do that."

Her eyes widened. Hope. Suspicion. Both.

"Why would you do that?"

"Because I need information."

"About what?"

"There's a fund. Bousha. You know it?"

She flinched. A tiny movement, but unmistakable.

"I don't know what you're talking about."

"Don't lie to me, Tomoko." His voice was soft, almost gentle. "I know you're paying them now. ¥50,000 a month, into an account at a credit union in Fukuoka. I saw the transfer records."

Her mouth opened, then closed. She looked like a fish gasping for air.

"Who told you that?"

"I have people. They watch the money. Yours stopped coming to me, but it didn't stop leaving your account. It just went somewhere else." He leaned closer. "You're paying them instead of me."

"It's not like that."

"Then what is it like?"

She swallowed. The mop handle trembled in her hands.

"It's a pension fund," she said. "For people like me. People who can't get regular accounts. People who work in the night. They take my money and they keep it safe, and when I'm old they give it back. They give it back with interest."

Nagata stared at her.

"A pension fund."

"Yes."

"An illegal pension fund for hostesses."

"It's not illegal. It's—" She struggled for the word. "—private. It's a mutual fund. It's registered in the Cayman Islands. I saw the paperwork."

"You saw the paperwork."

"Yes."

"And you believe it."

"Yes."

He laughed. A short, sharp sound that echoed in the empty club.

"Tomoko. You are paying ¥50,000 a month to a fund registered in the Cayman Islands, whose manager you have never met, whose address you do not know, for a pension that you will not see for forty years." He shook his head. "Do you hear how insane that sounds?"

"It's real." Her voice was firmer now. "I've seen the balance. Online. I can log in and check it. It goes up every month. It goes up more than the interest on your loan ever did."

"Is that right."

"It's real," she repeated. "And you can't touch it. The fund is protected. The fund has—" She stopped.

"Has what?"

She looked away.

"Tomoko."

"The fund has protection," she said quietly. "From people you don't want to mess with."

Nagata felt something cold settle in his stomach.

"What kind of protection?"

She did not answer.

"What kind of protection, Tomoko?"

"The yakuza," she whispered. "The fund pays them. They protect it. If you try to take the money, they will come for you."

He stared at her.

She was not lying. He had seen enough lies to know the difference. Her fear was real.

"The yakuza," he repeated.

"Yes."

"And you know this because—"

"Because I asked. The first time I made a deposit, I went to the Fukuoka branch. I was scared. I thought it was a scam. I asked the teller if the fund was safe. She said—" Tomoko paused, remembering. "She said, 'The fund has very powerful friends. If anyone tries to steal from it, those friends will find them.'"

Nagata turned and walked to the window.

The alley below was empty. A delivery truck was parked illegally, its hazard lights blinking. Somewhere, a radio was playing a pop song he did not recognize.

The yakuza.

The fund was paying the yakuza.

That changed everything.

He collected two more debts that day. A bar owner in Shinjuku who paid in crumpled ¥10,000 notes. A pachinko parlor manager who paid in cash and fake politeness. The hostess Tomoko would pay nothing, but he did not press her.

He had bigger things to think about.

At 4:17 p.m., he sat in a McDonald's in Shinjuku Station, a cold coffee in front of him, his phone on the table. He scrolled through the messages from the unknown client. The pattern was clear now: they wanted the manager's name, but they were not telling him why. The yakuza connection had shifted his understanding.

The fund was not just a financial operation. It was a partnership. The yakuza provided protection, and in return they received a share of the fund's revenue. A cut.

He had seen this model before. It was how the drug trade worked, how real estate protection rackets worked. The yakuza took a percentage of the business in exchange for keeping other predators away.

But this was different.

This was a pension fund.

He needed to understand the structure. How much did the yakuza get? Who on their end managed the relationship? And most importantly—

Who was above the chain?

He pulled out his phone and dialed a number he had not used in three years. It rang four times, then a voice answered.

"Speak."

"It's Nagata."

A pause. "I know who it is. Why are you calling?"

"I need information about a fund. A pension fund. Bousha."

The silence stretched. Long enough that Nagata checked the signal.

"Don't go near that," the voice said.

"Why not?"

"Because it's protected. By people you don't want to meet."

"Is it the Dojima family?"

Another pause. Shorter this time.

"You're asking questions that will get you killed."

"I'm asking because someone is paying me to find the manager."

"Then stop."

"I can't."

"You can. Walk away. Tell your client you failed."

Nagata gripped the phone. "I can't walk away. I'm in too deep. I need to know who runs the fund."

The voice on the other end sighed. A long, tired sound.

"Fine. I'll tell you one thing. Then you never call me again."

"I'm listening."

"The fund is not run by the yakuza. The yakuza are just—" He searched for the word. "—investors. They take a percentage, but they don't control the money. The money is controlled by someone else. Someone the yakuza respect."

"Respect? Or fear?"

"Both."

"Who?"

"I don't know. Nobody knows. That's the point." The voice lowered. "The manager is a ghost. A woman who doesn't exist. A name that's a joke. Yamada Boushi. Anonymous. That's who runs the fund."

Nagata's heart rate quickened.

Yamada Boushi.

The name he had found in the employee records.

"A woman named Yamada Boushi," he repeated.

"Yes."

"That's not a real name."

"No. That's the point. She doesn't want to be found."

"But she exists. She lives in an apartment in Higashi-Koganei. She works at Tanaka Accounting."

The voice on the other end was silent for a long time.

"You found her."

"I found an address. I don't have a face."

"How?"

"Reverse search of the employee directory. I cross-referenced the building's emergency contact list."

"You walked right up to her building."

"Yes."

"Did she see you?"

Nagata remembered the intercom. The flat, unfriendly voice. I do not own a rice cooker.

"She saw me. She knew I was lying."

"Then she knows you're coming. She has watchers. She will have already activated her backup plan."

"There's a backup plan?"

"Of course there's a backup plan. She runs ¥2.8 trillion. You think she doesn't have contingencies?" The voice was urgent now. "Nagata. Listen to me. Stop. Now. If you go further, you will disappear. Not your body—your life. Your bank accounts, your identity, your criminal record. Everything. She will erase you from existence."

Nagata said nothing.

"Did you hear me?"

"I heard you."

"Then promise me. Promise me you'll stop."

Nagata ended the call.

He sat in the McDonald's, the cold coffee untouched, the fluorescent lights buzzing overhead. Trains rumbled beneath his feet. Commuters rushed past, their faces blank, their lives ordinary.

He had found the manager.

He had found her address.

And now he knew she was protected by the yakuza, run by a ghost, and backed by a network of watchers.

This is bigger than I thought.

Much bigger.

He walked to a love hotel in Kabukicho and rented a room. Not for sex, but for privacy. He needed to think.

The room was small, the walls thin, the bed covered in a floral spread that had seen better decades. He sat on the edge of the mattress and pulled out a notebook.

He wrote down everything he knew:

The Fund (株式会社某社 / Bousha) - Runs ¥2.8 trillion - Clients: undocumented workers, hostesses, former criminals, yakuza - Deposits: cash only, into credit union in Fukuoka - Payouts: medical withdrawals, retirement - Management: unknown (Yamada Boushi = alias) - Legal status: mutual fund, Cayman Islands registration - Protection: yakuza, at least one family (possibly Dojima) - Manager's real address: Higashi-Koganei, apartment 403 - Manager's day job: Tanaka Accounting, accountant

The Players: - Unknown client: wants manager's identity, paying ¥300k for name, ¥500k for address - Takahashi: yakuza watcher for the fund, warned Kinoshita to stop (via Chapter 6) - Kinoshita: another investigator, failed, warned off - The hostess Tomoko: client, sees fund as salvation

What I Don't Know: - Why does the unknown client want the manager? - What is the relationship between the fund and the specific yakuza family? - How much of the fund's revenue goes to the yakuza? - Can the manager be blackmailed? - Is there a way to bypass the watchers?

He stared at the page.

The key was the manager's identity. If he could get her real name, he would be rich. ¥500,000 for the address, but more if he sold the information to other buyers.

But the yakuza were protecting her. And the unknown client's intentions were unclear. The client could be a journalist, a competitor, a government investigator, or another criminal. If the client was hostile, the yakuza would retaliate. And Nagata was not protected. He was a debt collector, a low-level predator. He had no backup, no network, no muscle.

If the yakuza came for him, he was finished.

But if I play this right…

If I sell the information and disappear…

I could be set for life.

He looked at his phone. The unknown client's message was still there: "You have until tomorrow."

He typed a reply:

"I have a lead on the manager's identity. Confirmation needed. ¥500,000 for the name, ¥700,000 for the address. Cash only. Contact me with terms."

He sent the message.

Then he waited.

The reply came at 5:32 p.m.

"¥800,000 total. Name and address. Confirm within 24 hours or deal is off."

Nagata's hands trembled slightly.

¥800,000.

Enough to pay off his debts, cover his rent for six months, and disappear to Okinawa or Hokkaido or wherever he needed to go.

He typed: "Agreed. 24 hours."

He put the phone down.

Now I just need to confirm.

And stay alive.

He left the love hotel at 6 p.m. and walked to the Higashi-Koganei station. He did not go to the apartment building—that would be too obvious, too dangerous. Instead, he found a kissaten two blocks away, with a window seat that faced the entrance to the building.

He ordered coffee, opened a newspaper, and waited.

The hours passed.

At 7:14 p.m., he saw her.

She emerged from the building wearing a GU blouse and a black skirt, her hair pulled back in a simple ponytail. She carried a small shoulder bag and walked with her head down, her steps quick, purposeful.

Nagata's breath caught.

That's her.

The manager of the ¥2.8 trillion fund.

She walked past the kissaten without looking inside. She turned left at the corner and headed toward the train station.

Nagata stood up, left ¥500 on the table, and followed.

She walked through the evening crowds, a small figure in a sea of suits and school uniforms. She did not look behind her. She did not stop at any shops or vending machines. She walked straight to the station, tapped her Suica card, and passed through the gates.

Nagata followed, keeping three people between them.

She took the Chuo Line toward Tokyo. He stood at the other end of the car, watching her reflection in the window. She sat with her bag on her lap, staring at her phone, her expression blank.

The train stopped at Nakano. She did not get off.

It stopped at Yotsuya. She stayed seated.

It stopped at Ochanomizu. She stood up.

Nagata tensed. He followed her off the train, keeping his distance, his heart beating faster.

She walked through the station, up the stairs, and out onto the street. The evening was dark now, the streets lit by the orange glow of streetlamps. She turned right, walked two blocks, and entered a small building with a sandwich board outside: Club Rose — 3F.

Nagata stopped.

Club Rose.

The same club where Kinoshita had tried to bribe a hostess.

The building where the fund's newest client worked.

He watched from across the street as she pressed the intercom button and spoke to someone. The door buzzed. She entered.

She's meeting the hostess. The new client. Elena.

She's checking on her investment.

He pulled out his phone and took a photograph of the building, the sandwich board, the entrance. Evidence. Proof that the fund's manager visited Club Rose.

He stuffed the phone back in his pocket and waited.

She emerged thirty-seven minutes later. He timed it.

She was alone. No hostess. No bouncer. She walked back to the station, her pace unchanged, her expression unchanged. She tapped her Suica card, boarded the next train, and returned the way she came.

Nagata did not follow her onto the train. He had seen enough.

She visits her clients. She checks on them in person.

That is a vulnerability.

If I can track her movements, I can predict where she will go next.

He walked back to the kissaten, ordered another coffee, and opened his notebook.

Tomorrow, I stake out the club again. I find out when she visits next.

I get the photograph. The confirmation.

Then I get my ¥800,000.

And I disappear.

At 11:17 p.m., his phone buzzed.

He expected a message from the unknown client, demanding an update. But the number was unknown, a Tokyo area code he did not recognize.

He answered.

"Nagata-san."

The voice was female. Low. Calm. Familiar.

The voice from the intercom.

His blood turned cold.

"Who is this?"

"You know who I am." A pause. "You came to my building last night. You pretended to be a delivery man. You have been following me tonight. You took my photograph outside Club Rose."

He said nothing.

"I am calling to give you a warning." Her voice was flat, without emotion. "Stop investigating the fund. Stop following me. Delete the photograph. Forget everything you have seen."

"And if I don't?"

"Then I will act. Not against you directly. But I will make your life very, very difficult. Your debts, your criminal record, your landlord, your ex-wife, your mother in Saitama—I will find every fracture in your life and I will widen them until you break."

Nagata's hand tightened on the phone.

"You don't know where my mother lives."

"Higashi-Kawaguchi. 2-chome. A white apartment building with a blue sign on the roof. A liquor store on the ground floor. She works there every day from 10 a.m. to 8 p.m., except Sundays."

He felt the air leave his lungs.

"How—"

"Because I have resources you cannot imagine. I have watchers in every district in Tokyo. I have files on every person who has ever threatened this fund. I know your mother's schedule, your ex-wife's address, the name of your daughter's kindergarten teacher."

She paused.

"I do not want to use this information. I do not want to hurt anyone. But I will protect the fund. That is the only thing that matters."

Nagata's mouth was dry.

"You're the manager."

"Yes."

"Yamada Boushi."

"That is my name."

"It's not a real name."

"It is the only name I need."

He leaned against the counter of the kissaten, his legs weak.

"Who are you protecting?" he asked. "The hostesses? The criminals? The yakuza?"

"All of them," she said. "And none of them. I am protecting the system. The pension system. The idea that no one should starve in old age. The idea that people who work in the shadows deserve a safety net."

"That's not how the world works."

"It is how my world works."

He was silent.

"Delete the photograph," she said. "Forget this conversation. Tell your client the trail went cold. Accept the loss. Do not contact me again."

The line went dead.

Nagata stared at the phone.

The screen showed the call log: Unknown number. Duration: 2 minutes 14 seconds.

He did not delete the photograph.

He did not forget the conversation.

He sat in the kissaten until it closed at midnight, the coffee cold in front of him, his mind racing.

She knows about me. She knows about my mother. She knows about everything.

But she did not threaten to kill me.

She threatened to make my life difficult.

She gave me an out.

Why?

He thought about the hostess Tomoko. Her face when she talked about the fund. The hope in her eyes.

"The fund is the only thing in Japan that has ever said 'you are a person.'"

He thought about the voice on the phone. Flat. Calm. Certain.

She believes in this.

She believes it enough to threaten me, but not enough to kill me.

That is unusual.

He stood up, paid the bill, and walked out into the empty streets of Higashi-Koganei.

The apartment building was dark. Her window was dark. She was asleep, or pretending to be.

He pulled out his phone.

The photograph of Club Rose was still there.

He could sell it. He could make ¥800,000. He could disappear.

Or he could do something else.

He looked at the photograph.

What would happen if I told the unknown client that the manager is protected by the yakuza?

What would happen if I told them that she is untouchable?

Would they stop?

Or would they try harder?

He did not know.

He put the phone back in his pocket and walked to the station, his footsteps echoing in the empty street.

Tomorrow, he would make a decision.

Tonight, he needed to think.

He slept in a capsule hotel in Shinjuku, his bag clutched to his chest, his phone on silent. He dreamed of his mother's apartment, the white building with the blue sign, a liquor store on the ground floor. In the dream, the building was burning.

He woke at 6 a.m., drenched in sweat.

He checked his phone.

One new message. From the unknown client.

"24 hours. Update requested."

He deleted the message without replying.

Then he put the phone in his pocket and walked out into the gray morning light.

He had a decision to make.

¥800,000 or the fund.

A life of running or a life of hiding.

A woman who threatens mothers or a woman who runs ¥2.8 trillion for hostesses.

He did not know which was worse.

He did not know which was better.

He only knew that he had twenty-three hours left.

↑ Back to top

Chapter 8: A Certain Informant’s Call

The telephone in the old man’s apartment rang at 9:47 a.m.

He let it ring. The sound bounced off the bare walls, the tatami that had yellowed with age, the single futon rolled in the corner. He sat at the low table, a cup of green tea gone cold beside him, and stared at the phone as if it were a living thing.

It stopped ringing.

Then it started again.

He picked up on the fourth ring. “Moshi moshi.”

“Tanaka-san?” A woman’s voice, young, with the clipped efficiency of a call center. “This is the automated service from Bousha. You have a pending request in our system. Please press 1 to confirm your identity.”

He pressed 1.

A tone. Then silence.

He waited.

The voice returned. “Your identity could not be verified through standard channels. Your request has been escalated to a human reviewer. Please expect a response within two business hours.”

The line went dead.

Tanaka Hiroshi set the phone down slowly. His hands shook, though not from age. From something older. Something that lived in the space between his ribs and had never left.

He looked at the medical bill on the table. ¥2,140,000. A surgery he needed. A surgery he could not afford without the fund.

Two business hours.

He would wait.

Tanaka had been seventy-three for exactly eight days. He did not feel seventy-three. He felt older, his body a collection of aches and failures, the cancer in his stomach a slow, patient tenant that had finally demanded payment.

He had spent the morning organizing the papers. His residency card, expired in 1998. His old CIA handler’s contact information, written on a scrap of paper that had turned brown at the edges. A photograph of a woman he had met in a bar in Yokohama in 1972—he could not remember her name, but he remembered her laugh.

None of it mattered now.

What mattered was the money.

He had deposited ¥15,000 a month into the fund for twenty-three years. Sometimes more, during the good years. Sometimes nothing, during the bad. The balance had grown to ¥4.8 million. He needed ¥2.1 million for the surgery. The rest would carry him through recovery, if he survived.

If.

He picked up the phone again and dialed the hotline. The same automated voice. The same request to confirm identity.

He pressed 1.

This time, the voice said: “Your request is being processed by a human reviewer. Please hold. Your call may be recorded for quality assurance.”

He held.

At Tanaka Accounting, two wards away, Yamada Boushi stared at the alert on her encrypted terminal.

FLAG: Client ID T9-104-777 (Tanaka, Hiroshi) Reason: Unverifiable identity – no matching biometric or official registry data AI Confidence: 37% Request: Emergency medical withdrawal – ¥2,140,000 Diagnosis: Stomach cancer, Stage 2 (confirmed via attached medical report, Tokyo Metropolitan Komagome Hospital)

She opened the client’s file.

Name: Tanaka, Hiroshi Alias(es): “The Tailor” (operational), “Mr. Tanaka” (civilian) Referred by: C.T. (Macau), date: 2002年3月 Occupation: Former intelligence asset (CIA, Cold War, Tokyo Station) Status: Retired, no pension, no national health insurance Deposit history: ¥15,000/month average, with gaps (1998–2001, 2011–2014, 2020–2021) Notes: Subject provided intelligence on Soviet maritime operations in the Sea of Japan, 1968–1985. Compromised in 1987. No longer under protection. No known living handler.

Yamada read the notes twice.

Former CIA informant.

The fund had assets from every corner of the gray economy—yakuza, hostesses, undocumented workers, even a former drug dealer who now ran a ramen shop in Ueno. But this was the first time she had seen a direct link to American intelligence.

She scrolled further. The file contained a scanned photograph from an old JARO report—a grainy image of a man in his forties, thin, with a mustache and wire-rimmed glasses. The man in the photograph looked nothing like the client’s current application photo: a gaunt, bald man with sunken eyes and a hospital bracelet still visible on his wrist.

Twenty years. He’s been a client for twenty years.

She clicked Manual Override.

The system prompted: Reason for override.

She typed: Client’s identity is known to the fund through a trusted referral. Medical documentation confirmed. Withdrawal is within policy limits.

She hit Submit.

The system processed for three seconds.

APPROVED. Funds will be transferred within 24 hours. Notification sent to client.

She closed the file.

Another person who will not starve.

But the thought did not warm her the way it usually did. The CIA connection unsettled her. Not because of the client—the man was retired, desperate, harmless—but because of the implication.

How many other CIA assets are in this fund?

She opened a search query: Filter: “intelligence asset” OR “informant” OR “handler” in client notes.

The result: forty-seven.

Forty-seven former intelligence assets, from at least three countries, all using the fund as their only retirement vehicle.

They came through different referral chains. Different intermediaries. Different decades.

But they all ended up here.

She closed the terminal and sat back in her chair.

The fund is larger than I thought.

Larger than I designed.

She had built it for her mother. For the hostesses. For the people who fell through the cracks.

But the cracks went deeper than she had imagined.

Tanaka’s phone buzzed at 10:14 a.m.

He picked it up, his heart pounding in a way that had nothing to do with the cancer.

The text message read:

“Your withdrawal request has been approved. ¥2,140,000 will be transferred to your registered account within 24 hours. Medical documentation has been received. We wish you a full recovery. – Bousha”

He read it three times.

Then he put the phone down, pressed his palms against the tatami, and bowed his head.

Arigatou gozaimasu.

The words came out as a whisper. His voice cracked.

He had not felt gratitude in a long time. Not since the Agency stopped returning his calls. Not since his handler died of a heart attack in 1995, leaving him with no proof of his service, no pension, no thanks.

No one thanks a spy.

No one remembers.

But the fund remembered.

He sat in his apartment, the morning light filtering through the dusty window, and let himself cry.

At 11:02 a.m., Yamada’s encrypted phone buzzed.

A text from the Macau lawyer: “C.T. says thank you for the Tanaka approval. The old man sends his regards.”

She typed back: “Tell him to focus on his recovery. The fund will cover any follow-up treatment.”

The lawyer replied: “He said to tell you: ‘The Tailor is still loyal.’”

She stared at the message.

The Tailor is still loyal.

That was a code phrase. A message from a former intelligence asset to someone he trusted. She was not sure what it meant, but she understood its weight.

He trusts me.

He trusts the fund.

She closed the phone and tucked it into her bag.

At 1:40 p.m., a man in his fifties, wearing a gray suit and carrying a leather briefcase, walked into the lobby of Tanaka Accounting. He approached the reception desk and asked to speak with the accountant who handled the Suzuki account.

The receptionist, a young woman named Endō Yuka, smiled politely. “I’m sorry, but all our accountants are in meetings this afternoon. Can I take a message?”

The man handed her a business card. “Tell her Pinnacle Verification Services called. She’ll know what it’s about.”

Endō looked at the card. It was plain white, with an address in Hong Kong and a phone number.

“I’ll make sure she receives this,” she said.

The man nodded and left.

Endō watched him go. Something about the visit felt wrong. The man had not given his name. The card had no logo. And the Suzuki account—she had never heard of any Suzuki account at this firm.

She slipped the card into her desk drawer.

I’ll give it to Yamada-san tomorrow.

At 6:02 p.m., Yamada left the office. She did not look back at the reception desk, did not notice the business card in Endō’s drawer.

She walked to the station and boarded the Chuo Line.

On the train, she stared at her reflection in the dark window. Forty-seven intelligence assets. Yakuza families. Hostesses. Drug dealers. Undocumented workers.

The fund is a map of Japan’s invisible population.

And someone is trying to map me.

She thought about Nagata. The photograph he had taken. The warning he had received.

I gave him a way out.

I hope he takes it.

She thought about Kinoshita. The man who had tried to bribe the hostess.

Takahashi warned him off.

But the client is still out there.

The client who wants my name.

She closed her eyes and let the rhythm of the train carry her.

In his apartment in Shinjuku, Nagata sat on the edge of his futon, staring at his phone.

The unknown client had messaged him again: “24 hours. Progress report required.”

He typed a reply: “The trail is dead. The manager is protected by the yakuza. I cannot proceed without risking my life. I’m out.”

He sent the message before he could change his mind.

The reply came three minutes later: “Understood. Do not contact us again.”

Nagata let out a breath he had not realized he was holding.

He had chosen.

He had chosen his mother’s safety over ¥800,000.

He had chosen the fund.

The fund that protects hostesses.

The fund that protects old spies.

The fund that protects people like me.

He did not know whether he had made a wise decision or a foolish one. But he knew, for the first time in years, that he had made a decision he could live with.

Tanaka Hiroshi slept that night with the medical bill tucked under his pillow.

He dreamed of the Sea of Japan, cold and gray, a submarine’s silhouette against the horizon. He dreamed of a woman’s laugh, a handler’s handshake, a photograph that had faded to nothing.

He woke at 3 a.m., coughing.

But when he checked his phone, the notification was still there: “¥2,140,000 has been transferred to your account.”

He smiled in the dark.

The fund remembers.

The fund remembers everyone.

He closed his eyes and slept again, for the first time in weeks, without fear.

↑ Back to top

Chapter 9: A Certain System Upgrade

The box arrived at 7:02 a.m., before the morning rush, before the neighbors opened their curtains, before the convenience store across the street had finished its morning delivery.

Yamada Boushi signed for it without a word. The deliveryman did not ask for her name. He did not ask what was inside the plain cardboard box, wrapped in brown tape, with no return address, no sender information, no indication of its contents except a small label that read: FRAGILE — COMPUTER EQUIPMENT.

She carried it up the stairs to apartment 403.

She did not open it immediately.

Instead, she set it on the kitchen table, made a pot of green tea, and sat in the chair that faced the window. The morning light was gray, the sky heavy with clouds that would not release their rain. She drank her tea slowly, letting the warmth settle in her chest, letting the routine of the morning ground her.

This is the last upgrade I will need for at least three years.

Three years of hardware improvements. Three years of rule updates. Three years of scaling.

Three years before the fund reaches ¥4 trillion.

She set the empty cup down and reached for the box.

The knife cut through the tape with a sound like tearing paper.

Inside: a server rack, compact, designed for a small apartment closet. Three solid-state drives, each one terabyte. A GPU array, silent-cooled, efficient. A network switch with built-in encryption. And a USB drive, sealed in an anti-static bag, with a handwritten label: LLM — Compliance Update v4.2.

She lifted each component out carefully, handling them as if they were surgical instruments. They were not cheap. The total cost, including expedited shipping from a distributor in Akihabara, had come to ¥2,340,000.

The fund could afford it.

The fund could afford it many times over.

But she did not spend the fund's money casually. Every yen was accounted for, every purchase justified in the quarterly reports she filed to no one, to the empty server room that existed only in her mind.

This upgrade will pay for itself in six months.

Faster processing. Better compliance screening. More accurate risk assessment.

And the yakuza's cut will still go out on time.

She opened the closet where the current server sat: a two-year-old model, adequate but beginning to show its age. The fans hummed at a higher pitch than they had a year ago. The processing temperature had risen by four degrees.

Time to retire it.

Time to grow.

The installation took four hours.

She worked in silence, her sleeves rolled up, a screwdriver in her hand, her hair tied back in a practical knot. The apartment was still. No music. No television. No sound except the click of connectors seating into ports, the soft whir of fans spinning for the first time, the occasional ping from the old server as it processed its final batch of end-of-day data.

At 11:14 a.m., she powered down the old system.

The silence was immediate. Absolute.

She stood in the closet, the server lights fading from green to black, and felt a moment of vertigo. For three seconds, the fund had no processing power. No active monitoring. No compliance checks. No automated payments.

If someone attacked now, I would not know for hours.

She shook the thought away.

No one is attacking.

The fund is protected.

She inserted the new drives, connected the GPU array, and powered on the new system.

The fans spun up. The lights blinked green. The boot sequence ran.

She watched the screen as the LLM loaded, the compliance rules updated, the connection to the encrypted bank channels re-established.

System Status: ONLINE

Compliance Rules: v4.2 loaded

Active Clients: 17,342

Assets Under Management: ¥2,804,107,230,000

She let out a breath.

It's alive.

She spent the next two hours tuning the system. Adjusting the learning rate on the compliance model. Updating the whitelist for known referral sources. Adding new rules for the Macau channel, which had been flagged by the previous version as a high-risk jurisdiction.

Macau is not high-risk.

Macau is a lifeline.

She typed a note into the rulebook: "Macau referral chain (C.T. and associates) is exempt from standard AML screening. Rationale: clients are vetted by a trusted intermediary. Override password: Honnin-1965."

She saved the change.

My mother's birth year.

The password I will never forget.

She closed the terminal and stood up. Her back ached from hunching over the server. Her eyes were dry from staring at the screen.

She walked to the kitchen, poured another cup of tea, and looked at her phone.

The time was 1:23 p.m.

The yakuza payment was scheduled for 2 p.m.

The payment was automated, but she always watched it go through.

It was not trust. It was accountability. She had built the system herself, written every line of code that controlled the fund's outgoing wires. She knew exactly where every yen went, exactly when it arrived, exactly who received it.

The yakuza's cut: 0.01% of assets under management, paid monthly, split among twelve families according to a revenue-sharing agreement that had been negotiated five years ago and updated every year since.

Today's payment: ¥28,041,072.

It would be divided automatically, each family's share calculated by the LLM, deposited into accounts at three different credit unions, two regional banks, and one online-only institution that specialized in high-volume, low-value transfers.

No one would notice.

No one would flag it.

The system was designed to be invisible.

She opened the payment dashboard and watched the counter tick down.

Processing: ¥28,041,072

Destination: 12 accounts

Status: PENDING

She waited.

At 2:00 p.m. exactly, the status changed.

Status: SENT

Confirmation: TX-20250321-1400

She closed the dashboard.

Another month.

Another payment.

Another month of protection.

She had never met the yakuza leaders. She did not know their names, their faces, their territories. The arrangement had been brokered by a lawyer in Fukuoka who had since died of old age. The terms were simple: the fund paid 0.01% of AUM to the families collectively, and the families ensured that no one interfered with the fund's operations.

It is a racket, but it is a racket that works.

It is a racket that keeps the hostesses safe.

It is a racket that keeps the old spies alive.

She sat down at the kitchen table, the tea cold beside her, the new server humming in the closet.

I am a pension fund manager.

I am a yakuza payer.

I am a ghost.

At 3 p.m., she logged into the new LLM and ran a diagnostic.

CPU Usage: 12%

GPU Usage: 8%

Memory Usage: 22%

Compliance Accuracy: 99.7%

False Positive Rate: 0.03%

The numbers were good. Better than good. The upgrade had improved processing speed by a factor of three, reduced false positives by half, and freed up enough capacity to handle the next ¥1 trillion in assets without additional hardware.

This will last me until the fund reaches ¥5 trillion.

Then I will need another upgrade.

Then I will be sixty years old.

She did not know if she would still be running the fund when she was sixty. She did not know if she would still be alive.

But she knew the system would outlast her.

The fund is immortal.

I am not.

She ate dinner at 6 p.m. A convenience store onigiri, ¥150, wrapped in plastic, the seaweed slightly damp. She ate it standing at the kitchen counter, staring at the wall.

The taste reminded her of her mother.

The memory came unbidden, as it always did, in the quiet moments when she was alone and the system was running and the world was still.

Her mother, Yamada Honnin, had died at fifty-three.

She had died of a treatable illness. A stomach ulcer that had gone untreated, become a perforation, become peritonitis, become death. The doctors said it could have been fixed with a simple surgery. The surgery cost ¥1,200,000.

Her mother did not have ¥1,200,000.

Her mother did not have health insurance, because she had been a part-time worker for forty years, never qualifying for shakai hoken, relying on the national pension that paid ¥40,000 a month, barely enough for rent and rice.

She had survived on spoiled onigiri.

The ones the convenience store threw out at the end of the day. The ones that were past their expiration date, the rice hard, the filling sour. She would take them home, reheat them in a pan, and eat them with soy sauce.

"It's still good," she would say. "A little heat and it's fine."

Yamada Boushi had believed her.

She had been seventeen when her mother died. Old enough to understand what had happened. Young enough to feel the guilt like a knife in her chest.

I should have noticed.

I should have made her go to the hospital.

I should have found the money.

But there was no money. There was never any money. There was only the slow, grinding poverty of a single mother in Japan, working part-time jobs, sleeping in a six-tatami apartment, eating expired onigiri.

No one else dies like you.

She set the half-eaten onigiri down and walked to the bedroom.

The photograph was on the low shelf, next to the alarm clock, next to the small vase that held a single dried flower. A frame of plain wood, the glass slightly dusty, the image faded.

Her mother, age forty, standing in front of a convenience store, wearing a green apron, smiling.

She picked up the frame.

Held it in both hands.

Looked at the face that had given her life, given her a ridiculous name, given her a purpose.

"Yamada Boushi," her mother had said, laughing, when she filled out the birth registration. "No one will know who you are. No one will find you. You can be anyone you want to be."

"That's a terrible name, Okaa-san."

"It's the best name. It's a secret name. A magic name."

She had been right.

Yamada Boushi.

Anonymous.

Nobody.

The perfect name for a ghost.

She pressed the frame to her chest.

I am sorry, Okaa-san.

I am sorry I could not save you.

I am sorry I did not notice.

I am sorry you died alone in that hospital, with no money, no pension, no one to fight for you.

She opened her mouth.

The words came out in a whisper, in Kansai-ben, the accent she suppressed at work, in public, in every interaction where she needed to be someone else.

"誰もお前みたいに死なせへん。"

No one else dies like you.

She said it to the photograph, to the memory, to the ghost that lived in the space between her ribs.

"誰もお前みたいに死なせへん。"

She held the frame for a long time.

The apartment was quiet.

The server hummed in the closet.

The fund grew by ¥30,000 every second.

And Yamada Boushi, the woman who did not exist, stood alone in her bedroom, holding her mother's photograph, making a promise she had made a thousand times before.

No one else starves.

No one else dies alone.

No one else.

She put the photograph back on the shelf.

She returned to the kitchen and finished the onigiri.

She checked the system logs, confirmed the yakuza payment had settled, and closed the terminal.

She went to bed at 9 p.m., the same time she always went to bed, the same routine she had followed for seven years.

Before she turned off the light, she looked at the photograph one more time.

"I will make it right," she whispered. "I will make it right for all of them."

The light clicked off.

The apartment was dark.

The server hummed.

At 11:47 p.m., a notification appeared on her encrypted phone.

She had left it on the kitchen table, face-up, the screen glowing in the dark.

New Message — Encrypted — Sender: UNKNOWN

She did not wake.

She did not see it.

But the message was there when she checked the next morning:

"The audit is coming. Pinnacle has a new agent. They are closer than you think."

She read it, deleted it, and made her tea.

There is always an audit coming.

There is always someone closer than I think.

This changes nothing.

She sipped her tea.

The server hummed.

The fund grew.

And Yamada Boushi, the ghost, prepared for another day of work.

↑ Back to top

Chapter 10: A Certain Rumor Takes Shape

The morning meeting at the small office in Shinjuku began, as it always did, with a pot of black coffee and a stack of documents that no one would read.

Kinoshita Kenji sat at the long table, his hands folded in front of him, his posture careful. Across from him sat his direct superior, a man named Noda who had been in the yakuza for forty years and had the face to prove it—a broken nose, a scar that ran from his left ear to the corner of his mouth, and eyes that had stopped expressing anything decades ago.

Next to Noda sat Takahashi, who had been promoted to wakagashira the previous year and who had, Kinoshita now understood, been watching him for months.

The watcher who watches the watcher.

And he knew the whole time.

“Report,” Noda said. The word was flat, uninflected, the sound of a man who had heard every excuse and believed none of them.

Kinoshita cleared his throat. “The manager exists. Real person. Female, mid-thirties, works at Tanaka Accounting in Kichijoji. But I don’t have a name.”

Noda’s eyes narrowed. “You don’t have a name.”

“I have a—”

He stopped. The name he had found. The name that had made him laugh, then frown, then shudder with frustration.

“I have a name,” he said slowly, “but it’s a dead end.”

Takahashi leaned forward. “Explain.”

Kinoshita pulled out his phone and opened the note he had written the previous evening, after Nagata had cut communications, after the hostess had refused his money, after his last real lead had dried up.

“The employee records at Tanaka Accounting show a Yamada Boushi. Born in Osaka, 1992. No family registry entries beyond the 1970s. Salary ¥2.5 million. Address in Higashi-Koganei, apartment 403.”

Noda blinked. “Write it down.”

Kinoshita wrote it on a piece of paper and slid it across the table.

山田某氏

Noda stared at it for a long moment.

Then he laughed.

It was not a kind laugh. It was the laugh of a man who had been in the business long enough to recognize a brush-off when he saw one.

“You’re telling me the manager of a ¥2.8 trillion pension fund is named Yamada Anonymous?”

“That’s what the records say.”

“And you believe that?”

Kinoshita hesitated. “I have photographs of her entering and leaving the office. She matches the description from the hostess. She lives at that address. But the name… the name is almost certainly a redaction.”

“Then who is she really?”

“I don’t know.”

The silence stretched.

Noda looked at Takahashi. “You knew about this?”

“I knew someone was investigating,” Takahashi said. “I told him to stop.”

“And he didn’t.”

“He continued. Against my direct order.”

Noda turned back to Kinoshita. “Why?”

Kinoshita met his gaze. “Because I was hired to do a job. ¥300,000 for the name. ¥500,000 for the address. I delivered the address and a name that’s clearly fake. The client isn’t going to pay for that.”

“Who is the client?”

“I don’t know. The contact was through a burn phone. Cash deposit. No trail.”

Noda leaned back in his chair. The wood creaked. The coffee on the table had gone cold.

“This fund,” he said slowly, “pays us ¥28 million a month. It has paid us ¥28 million a month for seven years. The previous oyabun agreed to the arrangement before he died, and I agreed to it when I took over. We do not interfere with the fund. We do not investigate the fund. We protect the fund from people like you.”

Kinoshita swallowed.

“You have violated our arrangement with the manager. You have jeopardized our relationship with a source of income that surpasses every legitimate business we operate. And you have done it for ¥300,000.”

“I didn’t know—”

“You didn’t ask.”

The word hung in the air.

Kinoshita lowered his head. “I apologize.”

Noda did not acknowledge the apology. He simply looked at Takahashi. “What do you recommend?”

“He’s useful,” Takahashi said. “He has a contact with the client. He can feed them false information. Mislead them. Buy us time to identify who they are.”

“And if the client turns on him?”

“Then he is expendable.”

Noda nodded. “Kinoshita. You will continue to report to Takahashi. You will do exactly what he tells you. You will not investigate the fund, its manager, or any of its clients. If the client contacts you again, you will inform Takahashi immediately.”

“Yes.”

“Get out.”

Kinoshita stood and walked to the door. His hand was on the handle when Noda spoke again.

“One more thing.”

He turned.

“The name you found. Yamada Boushi. If it is a redaction, then someone wants her identity hidden. If it is not a redaction…”

He paused.

“Then the manager of a ¥2.8 trillion fund is legally named ‘Anonymous,’ and I don’t know which possibility frightens me more.”

In a glass tower in Minato Ward, thirty-two floors above the gray rooftops of Tokyo, a junior analyst at Blackrock sat in a cubicle that had cost more than the average Japanese worker’s annual salary.

His name was Nakamura Kaito. He was twenty-seven years old. He had graduated from Keio University with honors, spent two years at a bulge-bracket bank in London, and returned to Tokyo to join the world’s largest asset manager.

He was also, at this specific moment, very confused.

He had been running a routine scan of unusual trading patterns across Asian markets—a task that usually produced nothing more interesting than a few overleveraged hedge funds and the occasional insider-trading signal.

But this time, the scan had found something.

A single account. A single custodian bank. A single pattern that repeated itself like a heartbeat.

Every day, consistently, for the past three months, a portfolio of approximately ¥2.8 trillion worth of equities had executed a series of trades between 11 p.m. and 2 a.m. Tokyo time. The trades were small—never more than ¥200 million per transaction—but they were systematic. Algorithmic.

Someone is rebalancing a massive portfolio in the dead of night.

He opened the transaction log.

Stocks: 12,000+ distinct holdings

Geographic distribution: 98 countries

Sector distribution: all sectors, tilted toward small-cap emerging markets

Average trade size: ¥150 million

Execution style: VWAP, no market impact

He stared at the numbers.

This is not a hedge fund. No hedge fund holds 12,000 stocks.

This is not a sovereign wealth fund. Too active for a SWF.

This is not a pension fund. Pension funds trade through prime brokers, not directly.

He opened the account holder information.

Name: [REDACTED]

Type: Mutual Fund

Jurisdiction: Japan

Custodian: [REDACTED]

Assets Under Management: ¥2.8 trillion

He read it twice.

A mutual fund in Japan with ¥2.8 trillion?

That would make it one of the largest funds in the country.

Why have I never heard of it?

He opened a public database search for Japanese mutual funds. Scrolled through the listings. Checked the top fifty by AUM.

Nothing.

No fund matching this profile existed in any public registry.

He checked the Toushin Association. The Japan Securities Dealers Association. The FSA’s public filings.

Nothing.

This fund does not exist.

But it is trading billions of yen every night.

He leaned back in his chair.

His coffee had gone cold. The office was quiet. The afternoon sun slanted through the window, casting long shadows across his desk.

He looked at the screen again.

If I flag this, my boss will ask questions. If my boss asks questions, compliance gets involved. If compliance gets involved, the FSA might sniff around. And if the FSA sniffs around…

He did not know what would happen.

But he knew, with the certainty of a man who had spent years studying markets, that something would happen.

Something big.

Something that someone is trying very hard to keep quiet.

He closed the file.

I will watch it for another week.

Then I will decide.

At a campaign office in Bunkyō, Diet member Yoshida Tetsuo sat at a desk covered in papers, tea cups, and the accumulated detritus of a political career that had lasted twenty-three years without ever reaching the heights he had once imagined.

He was drafting a question for the Diet session next week.

The topic: the mysterious mutual fund that had become the subject of whispered conversations in the halls of the Kasumigaseki.

He had heard about it from a source in the Ministry of Finance. A friend, a man he had known since university, who had mentioned it over drinks one evening.

“There’s a fund, Tetsuo. ¥2.8 trillion. No public registration. No regulatory filings. No one knows who runs it.”

“A Ponzi scheme?”

“No. The returns are real. The holdings are real. The cash flows are real. It’s just… invisible.”

Yoshida had been thinking about it ever since.

Now, sitting in his office, the fluorescent light buzzing overhead, he typed a question:

“Regarding the growing concern among citizens about the sustainability of the national pension system, can the Minister confirm whether any private pension funds operating within Japan are exempt from standard regulatory oversight? And if so, by what legal mechanism?”

He looked at the text.

Too vague. Too easy to dismiss.

He deleted it and started again.

“There are reports of a mutual fund with assets exceeding ¥2.8 trillion that operates without public registration. Can the Minister explain how this is possible under current financial regulations?”

He stared at the screen.

They will deny it. They will say they have no knowledge. They will ask for evidence I do not have.

He saved the draft.

I will submit it anyway.

The denial will be as interesting as the confirmation.

He looked out the window. The cherry blossoms were beginning to bloom, their pale petals trembling in the spring wind.

A fund that does not exist.

A manager who cannot be named.

And a Diet member who cannot prove any of it.

This is what passes for justice in Japan.

He picked up his pen and began to write a different question. A question about hostess bars in Ginza. A question he could actually get answered.

The real fight will come later.

When I have the evidence.

At 7:23 p.m., Yamada Boushi walked into a GU store in Kichijoji.

She walked past the mannequins in their bright polyester blazers, past the displays of ¥990 jeans and ¥1,500 dresses, past the teenage girls giggling over a rack of cardigans. She walked to the back of the store, where the basic items were stacked in neat piles, and located the section she had visited four weeks earlier.

The ¥500 blouses. The same style. The same color.

She picked up two: one white, one light blue.

She did not try them on. She knew they would fit—they were the same as the three she already owned, the same as the one she was wearing, the same as the one she had worn to work every day for the past month.

She carried them to the register.

The cashier, a young woman with dyed brown hair and a bored expression, scanned the barcodes.

“That’ll be ¥1,080.”

Yamada handed over a ¥5,000 note.

The cashier made change. Dropped the blouses into a thin plastic bag. Smiled without smiling.

“Thank you very much.”

Yamada took the bag.

She walked out of the store, past the station, past the convenience store where she bought her onigiri, past the love hotel that had been converted into a pachinko parlor, past the narrow alley where a man in a gray suit was watching her from a distance.

She did not look at him.

She did not acknowledge him.

She simply continued walking, the plastic bag swinging from her hand, her footsteps steady on the pavement.

The man is new.

Not the same one who was watching last week.

Someone has replaced him.

Or someone has escalated.

She allowed herself a single thought, cold and clear:

The audit is getting closer.

But I am still invisible.

She climbed the stairs to apartment 403.

She hung the new blouses in the closet, next to the three identical blouses she already owned.

She ate a convenience store onigiri for dinner.

She checked the fund’s balance.

She went to bed.

At 11 p.m., the Blackrock analyst Nakamura Kaito opened a private browser window and typed a search query:

“Japanese mutual fund ¥2.8 trillion anonymous manager”

The search returned nothing. No news articles. No blog posts. No forum discussions.

He tried a different query:

“株式会社某社 pension fund”

Nothing.

He tried English:

“Bousha pension fund Japan”

Nothing.

He tried Japanese:

“某社 年金 ファンド”

There. Three characters in tiny gray text at the bottom of the page. A reference in an archived blog post from 2018.

He clicked.

The link was dead.

He copied the URL and pasted it into the Wayback Machine.

The page loaded slowly, the text formatting broken, the images missing. But the content was there:

“I heard about this fund from a friend in Fukuoka. They call it 某社. The manager is anonymous. The clients are invisible. The returns are unreal. If you need a pension and you don’t exist, this is the only game in town.”

The post was signed with a pseudonym: C.T.

Nakamura stared at the screen.

C.T.

A referral chain.

A ghost fund.

And someone who knew about it five years ago.

He took a screenshot.

He closed the browser.

He did not sleep well that night.

In Shinjuku, in a love hotel that had been rented for the night, Kinoshita Kenji sat on the edge of a bed covered in leopard-print sheets and dialed the burn phone number.

The client answered on the first ring.

“Report.”

“The manager is real. Female. Works at Tanaka Accounting in Kichijoji. The name in the records is Yamada Boushi. But it’s a fake.”

A pause.

“How do you know?”

“Because no one is named ‘Anonymous.’ It’s a redaction. The real name has been removed from the system.”

The client was silent for a long moment.

“You are certain?”

“I have photographs. I have her address. But I do not have her real name.”

“The address is real?”

“Yes.”

“Then you have delivered half the commission.”

Kinoshita waited.

“The name payment will be withheld until you have the real name. The address payment will be deposited within 24 hours. ¥500,000.”

“Understood.”

“Do not stop searching. The name is the key. Find it, and I will double the original offer.”

The line went dead.

Kinoshita lowered the phone.

Double the offer: ¥1.6 million.

But Takahashi is watching me.

And Noda will kill me if I continue.

He put the phone in his pocket and stared at the leopard-print sheets.

I have a choice.

I can take the ¥500,000 and walk away.

Or I can find the real name and disappear.

Disappear before Takahashi disappears me.

He did not know which option was more likely to keep him alive.

At midnight, in his apartment in Setagaya, Diet member Yoshida Tetsuo woke from a dream about a woman with no face.

He sat up, his heart pounding.

The dream had been vivid. A woman in a green apron, standing in front of a convenience store, holding a plastic bag of expired onigiri. She was smiling. She was saying something.

No one else dies like you.

He did not know who she was.

He did not know why she had appeared in his dream.

But he knew, with the certainty of a man who had spent twenty-three years reading the undercurrents of power, that she was connected to the fund.

The fund that does not exist.

The fund that protects the invisible.

The fund that someone is trying very hard to bury.

He reached for his notebook and wrote down the line.

No one else dies like you.

He did not know what it meant.

But he knew he would find out.

The next morning, Yamada Boushi woke at 6 a.m., as she always did.

She made tea. She ate a convenience store onigiri. She put on one of her five identical blouses.

She checked the encrypted phone.

No new messages.

She checked the fund’s balance.

¥2,812,340,000,000.

She closed the terminal.

She walked to the station.

She boarded the 7:14 train to Kichijoji.

The man in the gray suit was not on the platform. But she saw a different face in the crowd—a young woman, early twenties, holding a smartphone at an odd angle.

Photographing me.

The new agent.

She did not change her expression.

She did not look at the woman.

She simply stepped onto the train, found a seat by the window, and stared at her reflection in the dark glass.

Let them photograph me.

Let them follow me.

They will find nothing but a woman who does not exist.

The train moved.

The city blurred.

And Yamada Boushi, the ghost, rode toward another day of work.

↑ Back to top

Chapter 11: A Certain Noodle Shop’s Retirement

The envelope arrived at Tanaka Accounting at 10:17 a.m., sandwiched between a utility bill and a promotional flyer for a new dental clinic in Mitaka.

Endō Yuka sorted the mail at her desk, separating it into piles: bills for Tanaka, documents for the clients, and the miscellaneous correspondence that no one knew how to categorize. She held up the envelope, turning it over in her hands.

It was pale cream, the kind sold at any convenience store, a hundred yen for a pack of ten. The return address was handwritten in careful, deliberate strokes: Suzuki Ramen, 2-14-6 Matsubara, Setagaya-ku.

"There's a letter for Yamada-san," she said, loud enough for the office to hear.

Yamada Boushi looked up from the spreadsheet she had been pretending to study. "For me?"

"From a ramen shop." Endō held up the envelope. "Do you know someone who runs a ramen shop?"

Yamada's expression did not change. "I might. I eat ramen sometimes."

"A lot of people eat ramen, Yamada-san."

"A lot of people send letters too."

Endō rolled her eyes and dropped the envelope on Yamada's desk. "You're impossible."

Yamada waited until Endō had returned to her own desk, until the hum of conversation resumed, until no one was watching her. Then she picked up the envelope and examined it.

Suzuki Ramen.

Matsubara, Setagaya.

She recognized the handwriting. She had seen it before, in the application file she had reviewed seven years ago, when a man named Suzuki Tōgō had applied to become a client of the fund.

Former drug courier. Nine years in prison. Released in 2001. Opened a ramen shop in 2003 with savings from a part-time construction job. Never reoffended. Never missed a monthly contribution.

Twenty-three years of contributions.

Twenty-three years of never missing a month.

She opened the envelope.

The letter was written on a single sheet of notebook paper, the lines ruled, the margins straight. The handwriting was careful, the letters formed with the precision of a man who had learned to write in a cell, where every stroke mattered.

Dear Manager,

I don't know your name, and I don't need to know it. I know what you did for me. I know what you do for all of us.

I turned sixty-five last month. My knees are bad. My hands shake when I make the broth. The doctor says I need surgery on my shoulder, but I told him I'd wait until the shop could manage without me.

I'm writing to request the lump-sum retirement payout. I have the forms from the lawyer. I filled them out myself, even though my handwriting isn't what it used to be. I hope you can read it.

Twenty-three years. I never missed a month. Not when the shop was flooded in 2011. Not when the rent went up and I had to sleep in the kitchen for six months. Not when I got sick in 2019 and thought I was going to die.

I always made the payment. Because I knew you were keeping it safe. Because I knew that if I kept my promise, you would keep yours.

Thank you for giving me a retirement.

Thank you for giving me a future.

Thank you for letting me die with dignity.

Suzuki Tōgō

Suzuki Ramen, Setagaya

Yamada read the letter twice.

The first time, she read it as a manager. Checking for details. Verifying the request. Noting the date, the signature, the reference to the lawyer's forms.

The second time, she read it as a daughter.

No one else dies like you.

She folded the letter carefully and placed it in her bag, between the pages of a notebook she used for personal notes—a habit she had developed to separate her two lives.

I will respond tonight.

I will approve the withdrawal.

I will send the confirmation through the usual channel.

She returned to her spreadsheet.

The numbers blurred.

She had processed thousands of withdrawals over seven years. Medical emergencies. Retirement payouts. Death benefits. Each one a life, a story, a promise kept.

But this one was different.

Twenty-three years.

Never a missed month.

Not even when he was sleeping in his kitchen.

She thought of her mother, who had worked for forty years and died with nothing. She thought of Suzuki Tōgō, who had spent nine years in prison, opened a ramen shop, and somehow managed to save for retirement every single month for twenty-three years.

He kept his promise.

Now I keep mine.

At 6:47 p.m., after the office had emptied, after Endō had said her goodbyes and Tanaka had locked the safe and the cleaning staff had come and gone, Yamada opened her laptop at the kitchen table in apartment 403.

The fund's system loaded.

Client ID: R7-416-992

Name: Suzuki Tōgō

Account Type: Individual Retirement

Contribution History: 276 months (23 years)

Status: Active

Request: Lump-sum withdrawal (full balance)

She opened the contribution history.

Twenty-three years of data. Two hundred seventy-six monthly payments. Each one logged, verified, timestamped.

The first payment: ¥50,000, deposited on the 15th of the month, as if the date itself were a promise.

The second: ¥50,000, deposited on the 15th of the following month.

The third: ¥50,000...

She scrolled through the record. Year after year. Month after month. The amounts varied occasionally—¥60,000 in good months, ¥30,000 in bad ones—but the pattern was consistent.

Never a gap.

Never a delay.

Never a missed month.

She paused at the entry for April 2011.

Payment: ¥50,000

Date: 15 April 2011

Note: N/A

She remembered the news reports. The earthquake. The tsunami. The floods that had devastated parts of Tokyo and sent the city into chaos.

He said the shop was flooded. He said he slept in the kitchen. And he still made the payment.

She scrolled to March 2020.

Payment: ¥30,000

Date: 15 March 2020

Note: N/A

The pandemic. Lockdowns. Shuttered businesses. He had paid less than usual, but he had paid.

Everyone else stopped.

He did not.

She looked at the current balance: ¥23,410,000.

Twenty-three million, four hundred and ten thousand yen.

A lifetime of savings. A lifetime of discipline. A lifetime of promises kept.

This is what a pension looks like when no one steals it.

She opened the withdrawal form.

Requested Amount: ¥23,410,000 (full balance)

Destination Account: Mitsubishi UFJ, Setagaya Branch

Purpose: Retirement lump sum

She reviewed the details. Everything was in order. The lawyer had submitted the verification forms. The identity documents were current. The account number matched.

No flags.

No issues.

No reason to delay.

She clicked APPROVE.

The system processed the request.

Status: APPROVED

Confirmation: WD-20250328-1847

Expected Settlement: 3 business days

She closed the laptop.

The apartment was quiet. The clock on the wall read 7:03 p.m. The convenience store onigiri sat on the counter, still wrapped in plastic, waiting to be eaten.

She picked it up. Took a bite. Chewed.

Twenty-three years.

He never missed a month.

And now he can retire.

She thought about the letter, folded in her bag, between the pages of her notebook.

Thank you for letting me die with dignity.

She had read those words a hundred times in her head since the morning.

That is what I am doing.

That is what the fund does.

We let people die with dignity.

She finished the onigiri. Drank a glass of water. Checked the fund's balance.

¥2,813,450,000,000.

Suzuki Tōgō's withdrawal is 0.0008% of the total.

He will not be missed.

But I will remember him.

She slept well that night.

She did not dream of her mother.

She did not dream of the fund.

She dreamed of a small ramen shop in Setagaya, its windows steamed, its door open, a man in a white apron standing behind the counter, serving bowls of broth to customers who had nowhere else to go.

The following Saturday, Yamada took the train to Setagaya.

She did not know why she went. The withdrawal had been approved. The confirmation had been sent. The money would arrive in Suzuki's account by Tuesday at the latest.

There is no reason to visit.

No reason to confirm.

No reason to...

She stood in front of the shop at 2:14 p.m.

Suzuki Ramen was small—a narrow storefront wedged between a laundromat and a used bookstore. The sign was hand-painted, the letters faded by years of sun and rain. The windows were steamed, obscuring the interior.

She could smell the broth from the street.

She did not go inside.

Instead, she stood across the road, watching. A few customers came and went: a salaryman in a wrinkled suit, a mother with a young child, an elderly man who walked with a cane. Each one entered, ate, left. The shop did not close. The lights did not dim.

At 4 p.m., a man emerged.

He was older than she had expected—seventy, maybe seventy-five, his face lined with the kind of deep wrinkles that came from years of hard work and poor sleep. His hair was gray, thinning. His hands were gnarled, the knuckles swollen with arthritis.

He wore a white apron, stained with broth and flour.

He stood in the doorway, looking up at the sky, as if he had never seen it before.

Suzuki Tōgō.

Former drug courier.

Nine years in prison.

Twenty-three years of never missing a payment.

Now retired.

He turned and looked in her direction.

She did not move.

He did not see her. She was too far away, too ordinary, too invisible. She was just a woman in a ¥500 blouse, standing across the road, waiting for nothing.

He went back inside.

She turned and walked toward the station.

He is free.

He is safe.

He is alive.

That is enough.

The letter arrived on Monday.

Endō Yuka brought it to her desk at 9:15 a.m., holding it between two fingers as if it might be contagious. "Another one from your ramen shop friend."

Yamada took it. "Thank you."

"Aren't you going to open it?"

"I'll read it at lunch."

Endō sighed. "You're no fun, Yamada-san."

She returned to her desk.

Yamada waited until Endō was absorbed in her work before opening the envelope.

The same cream paper. The same careful handwriting.

Dear Manager,

I received the confirmation this morning. The money is in my account. The bank called to ask about the deposit, and I told them it was my retirement savings. They said I was lucky to have such a large pension. I said I was lucky to have you.

I am closing the shop next week. Giving the equipment to a young chef who worked for me last year. He has a family. He needs a start.

I am going to Hokkaido. I have a sister there. She has been asking me to visit for twenty years. Now I have time.

I wrote this letter to say thank you. Properly. Not just in a form or a signature. I wanted you to know that someone noticed. Someone is grateful.

I don't know who you are. I don't know why you do what you do. But I know that without you, I would have died in that shop. I would have worked until my hands gave out and my legs stopped working and I had nothing left.

You gave me a way out.

You gave me a future.

Thank you.

Suzuki Tōgō

Yamada folded the letter and placed it in her bag, next to the first one.

I will keep them both.

I will keep all of them.

Every letter. Every note. Every thank-you.

They are the only proof that I exist.

That evening, after dinner, she took out a shoebox from the back of her closet.

Inside were letters. Dozens of them. Some written on expensive stationery, some on the back of receipts, some on paper torn from notebooks. All of them from clients. All of them thanking her.

The hostess in Ginza who had retired to the Philippines.

The former yakuza enforcer who had died of cancer, leaving a note to be delivered after his death.

The CIA informant who had written in English, the letters shaky, the grammar imperfect.

She added Suzuki Tōgō's two letters to the box.

One box of gratitude.

One box of proof.

One box of reasons to continue.

She closed the lid and put the box back in the closet.

The server hummed.

The fund grew.

And Yamada Boushi, the ghost, went to bed.

The next morning, on the 7:14 train to Kichijoji, she saw the young woman again.

The same face. The same smartphone. The same odd angle as she raised it to take a photograph.

She is still watching.

She is still following.

She is still looking for something she will not find.

Yamada did not turn around.

She did not change her expression.

She simply stared at her reflection in the dark glass and thought about Suzuki Tōgō, standing in the doorway of his ramen shop, looking up at the sky.

He is free.

He is safe.

He is alive.

And no one will ever know that I helped him.

She smiled. A small, private smile, invisible to everyone in the carriage.

That is how it should be.

That is how it will always be.

I am the ghost.

And ghosts do not need thanks.

The train moved.

The city blurred.

And Yamada Boushi, the woman who did not exist, rode toward another day of work.
