Chapter 12: A Certain Debt Collector’s Investigation

[WARNING: This chapter did not pass critic review. Review manually.]

Chapter 12: A Certain Debt Collector's Investigation

The debt collection agency in Ikebukuro occupied the third floor of a building that had not been renovated since the bubble economy. The ceiling tiles were stained brown from a leak that no one had ever fixed. The fluorescent lights buzzed continuously, a low hum that had become part of the background noise of daily existence.

Nagata Shōgo sat at his desk, staring at the blank wall.

He had not touched the investigation in five days.

The photograph of Yamada Boushi was still on his phone, but he had moved it into a locked folder. The notes about the fund, the address in Higashi-Koganei, the memory of the warning call—all of it had been buried under a pile of new debt collection cases. Small cases. Safe cases.

Stay away. Protect your mother. Let it go.

He had told the client he was out. He had deleted the burner app. He had promised himself that he would forget.

But the curiosity remained, a low-grade fever that never quite broke.

Who is she? How does she do it? Why does she do it?

He shook his head and reached for a case file.

It doesn't matter. I have a job. I have a mother. I have a life.

I can't afford to be curious.

At noon, Nagata went to the convenience store on the ground floor of his building. He picked up a bento and a can of coffee, paid, and walked back toward the elevator.

That was when he saw the man.

He was standing near the entrance of the building, leaning against a black sedan. He was in his early fifties, with a scar running from his left eyebrow to his jaw. He wore a dark suit that did not fit well and sunglasses that did not hide his direction of gaze.

He was looking at Nagata.

Nagata kept walking.

Don't react. Don't show fear. Just go back to the office.

He made it to the elevator. Pressed the button. The doors opened.

A hand caught the door before it closed.

The scarred man stepped inside.

"Nagata-san."

Nagata's throat went dry. "Who are you?"

"I'm a friend of the fund." The man's voice was low, almost gentle. "My name is Takahashi. I work for Noda-san. You've been busy lately."

"I'm out. I told the client I was out."

"That's not how these things work." The elevator doors closed. The car began to rise. "You were paid. You delivered information. Then you tried to walk away. The fund doesn't let people walk away until the debt is settled."

"I don't owe anything."

"You owe your mother's safety."

Nagata's blood froze.

He knows about my mother.

They all know.

"What do you want?"

"Stop investigating. Stop digging. Forget everything you learned."

"I already tried. Someone else contacted me. Someone from Pinnacle. They threatened my mother."

"I know." Takahashi's eyes narrowed. "That's why I'm here. The fund's enemies are moving. They tried to use you. They will try again. But if you stay out, your mother stays safe."

"And if I don't?"

Takahashi didn't answer. He just looked at Nagata with an expression that held no threat, only certainty.

"You have seventy-two hours to disappear from this case. After that, Noda-san will consider you a liability."

The elevator stopped at the third floor. The doors opened.

Takahashi stepped out first. He turned back.

"Seventy-two hours. Use them wisely."

He walked down the hallway and disappeared into the stairwell.

Nagata stood frozen in the elevator, the bento and coffee growing cold in his hands.

He did not go back to his desk.

He went straight to the station, bought a ticket for Shinjuku, and called the only person he could think of.

Elena did not pick up.

He left a voicemail: "It's Nagata. I need to see you. It's urgent."

He waited on the platform, watching the trains come and go.

Seventy-two hours.

I have three days.

And then what? Disappear?

I can't disappear. I have a mother. I have a job. I have a life.

And I have a name to clear.

The train arrived. He stepped on.

Club Rose was dark when he arrived. The neon sign was off. The door was locked.

He knocked anyway.

After a long minute, the door cracked open. Elena's face appeared, tired and wary.

"I told you not to come back."

"I know. But things have changed."

"Things have not changed. The fund does not change. The manager does not change."

"Takahashi visited me today. He gave me seventy-two hours to disappear."

Elena's expression flickered. "Then take the offer. Go. For your mother's sake."

"I can't. Someone from Pinnacle threatened my mother first. They told me I have fourteen days to find the manager's real name and the server location. If I don't, they will kill her."

Elena was silent.

"Who is behind Pinnacle?"

"I don't know. No one knows. That's why they are dangerous."

"Then how do I protect my mother?"

"You don't. You disappear. You take your mother and you leave Tokyo. You go somewhere no one can find you."

"I can't afford that."

"Then you are already dead."

Nagata felt the weight of her words.

She is right.

I am out of options.

Unless…

"Elena, I need information. The name of the person behind Pinnacle. The real client."

"I already told you, I don't know."

"Then help me find out. You have connections. You have access. You know people who know people."

Elena stared at him for a long moment.

"I can't give you the manager's name. I don't know it. No one knows it. But I can give you a way to find the truth."

"How?"

"Go back to the beginning. The audit request that started this. The fake Pinnacle Verification Services letter. It was sent from a shell company in Hong Kong. Someone in Tokyo authorized it. Someone with links to the government, the underworld, or both."

"How do I find that someone?"

"Trace the signature. The letter was signed by a man named Matsumoto Tatsuya. Former Ministry of Justice accountant. He disappeared from the registry a year ago. But he still collects mail at a convenience store in Yokohama."

Nagata's heart beat faster.

Matsumoto Tatsuya.

A name.

"A convenience store?"

"Kamiōoka, near the station. Number 14. Check the mailbox. If he is still collecting, he will lead you to the client."

"How do you know this?"

"I used to work for the fund's intelligence arm. Before I became a hostess. The manager taught me to always have a backup."

Nagata nodded slowly.

"I owe you."

"You owe me nothing. Just don't get killed."

He took the train to Yokohama the same afternoon.

The convenience store was in a quiet residential area, far from the station. A middle-aged woman in an apron was stocking shelves. The mailboxes were in a narrow alcove near the entrance.

Number 14. Matsumoto Tatsuya.

Nagata crouched down and examined the lock. It was cheap, the kind that could be opened with a paperclip in thirty seconds.

He looked around. No cameras visible. No employees watching.

He pulled out a paperclip and went to work.

Twenty seconds later, the lock clicked open.

Inside the mailbox was a single envelope, unmarked. Nagata pulled it out and examined it.

Postmarked three days ago. No return address.

He opened it carefully.

Inside was a single sheet of paper. A business card was taped to the page.

The card read: Takeda Ryōzō, Attorney at Law. Marunouchi, Chiyoda-ku. 15F, Fuji Building.

Below the card, handwritten in black ink: "Next payment due December 15. ¥500,000. Same account."

Nagata's hands trembled.

Takeda Ryōzō.

A lawyer.

A payment link.

This is the chain.

He folded the paper and the card into his pocket.

Now I have a name.

Now I can trace the threat.

And now I need to decide who to trust.

He did not go home.

Instead, he went to Higashi-Koganei.

The apartment building was dark, the windows blank. He climbed the stairs to the third floor, then the fourth.

Apartment 403.

The door was closed. A small plaque near the buzzer read YAMADA in faded black ink.

He stood there, staring at the door.

She is in there.

The manager.

The woman who warned me.

The woman who threatened my mother to protect herself.

He had hated her for that. He had feared her. He had wanted to destroy her.

But now he understood.

She did it to survive.

She is not the enemy.

The enemy is the one who hired me.

The one who threatened my mother.

The one who is using Pinnacle to destroy the fund.

He pulled out a piece of paper and a pen. He wrote:

"I know about Takeda Ryōzō. I know about the payment schedule. I know about Pinnacle. I was hired to find you. I failed. Now someone else wants me to try again. My mother is in danger.

I do not trust you. But I cannot fight this alone. I need your help to expose the real threat.

Meet me at the park near Kichijoji Station. Midnight. Come alone.

If you do not come, I will take what I know to the police. And then we both lose."

He hesitated.

She threatened my mother.

She is not my ally.

But she is the only one who can stop this.

He folded the note and slid it under the door.

Then he turned and walked away.

She will find me.

She always finds the people who are looking.

And tonight, I am looking for the truth.

Chapter 13: A Certain Informant's Illness

The clock on the bedside table read 3:14 a.m.

Tanaka Hiroshi lay in the dark, staring at the ceiling. His stomach was a knot of pain that no medicine could untie. The doctors at the university hospital had been kind, but kindness could not stop cancer.

Stage three.

Six months, maybe less.

If the treatment works.

He had received the medical withdrawal five weeks ago. The money had arrived in his account. He had paid his bills, bought his medication, and written a thank-you letter to the manager—a woman whose face he had never seen, whose name he did not know.

She had approved the withdrawal. She had overridden the AI flag. She had kept her promise.

I should be grateful.

I am grateful.

But gratitude does not cure cancer.

He reached for the glass of water on the bedside table. His hand trembled. The glass slipped.

It shattered on the floor.

He lay still, listening to the silence of the apartment. The neighbors would not complain. They never did. No one visited. No one called.

This is what dying alone looks like.

He forced himself to sit up. The room swayed. He waited for his vision to clear, then swung his legs over the edge of the bed.

The glass fragments glinted in the weak light from the window.

I'll clean it later.

I'll clean it when I can stand.

I'll clean it when the pain stops.

He picked up his phone. The screen glowed dimly.

Date: February 10, 2025

Time: 3:17 a.m.

He opened the fund's portal—a simple web interface, no branding, no logo, just a login screen with a single input field. He typed his client ID: T9-104-777.

The screen changed.

Welcome, Tanaka Hiroshi.

Account Balance: ¥3,847,200

Next Contribution: N/A (Medical Withdrawal Active)

Status: Active - Under Medical Observation

He stared at the number.

Three million, eight hundred forty-seven thousand, two hundred yen.

Not enough to live on.

Enough to die with.

He had contributed for fourteen years. Fourteen years of sending money to an account he had never seen, managed by a person he had never met. He had done it because the CIA handler had told him to.

"This fund will take care of you when I cannot."

The handler's words echoed in his memory.

Onoda Eiji.

That was his name.

A man in a gray suit who visited once a month for five years, bringing cash and questions. A man who had promised Tanaka that if he kept his mouth shut and his payments consistent, he would never have to worry about retirement.

"You served your country. Now your country will serve you."

He lied.

The fund did not lie. The fund kept its promise. But the handler lied.

He said the government would take care of me.

He said the CIA would protect me.

He said I would never be alone.

And then he disappeared.

Tanaka closed the portal and opened his photo album.

There was one photograph of Onoda Eiji, taken in 1978. Tanaka had stolen it from the handler's desk during a meeting—a small risk, a necessary insurance. The photograph showed a young man in his thirties, clean-shaven, with a crew cut and the unblinking eyes of a professional.

The same eyes that had watched Tanaka's first mission. The same eyes that had evaluated his loyalty. The same eyes that had looked away when the agency cut ties in 1985.

"You are a liability, Hiroshi. Your asthma is too severe for field work. We are reassigning you to a desk job in Tokyo."

"Desk job?"

"You will receive a monthly stipend. You will report to a handler named Tanaka. You will continue to provide intelligence on the Japanese banking sector."

"I don't understand."

"You are being retired. Quietly. Discreetly. You will never speak of your past. You will never contact anyone from your former cell. You will live a normal life and forget that any of this happened."

"And if I refuse?"

"Then you become a target."

Tanaka had accepted the desk job. He had reported to a handler named Tanaka—a man who shared his surname but not his blood. He had provided intelligence on the Japanese banking sector for nine years, until the handler himself was retired in 1994.

After that, the stipends stopped.

The promises stopped.

The fund was the only thing that remained.

He scrolled through the photograph album.

There were other images: his mother, long dead; his father, who had disowned him after the arrest; the apartment in Shinjuku where he had lived for twenty years; the hospital room where he had received the diagnosis.

No wife.

No children.

No one to remember me.

He stopped at a photograph from 1982. He was standing in a park in Yokohama, next to Onoda Eiji. The handler's arm was around his shoulder. They were both smiling.

That was before the betrayal.

Before the desk job.

Before the disease.

He zoomed in on the handler's face.

Where are you now, Onoda-san?

Are you alive?

Do you remember me?

Do you remember your promise?

He closed the photo album and opened a text document.

Draft - Unsent Letter

To: The Fund Manager

From: Tanaka Hiroshi, Client ID T9-104-777

Date: February 10, 2025

He stared at the blank screen.

What do I say to someone who saved my life?

What do I say to someone whose name I do not know?

He began to type.

Dear Manager,

I received the withdrawal five weeks ago. The money is in my account. I have started the chemotherapy. The doctors say there is a chance. A small chance, but a chance.

I am writing to thank you. I have no other words. Just thank you.

I do not know who you are. I do not know why you do what you do. But I know that without you, I would have died in debt. I would have spent my last months worrying about money instead of fighting for my life.

You gave me a gift. You gave me time.

I have been thinking about my life. All the choices I made. All the people I disappointed. All the promises I broke.

I was an informant for the CIA in the 1970s. I provided intelligence on Japanese banking reforms. I thought I was serving my country. I thought I was doing the right thing.

When they were done with me, they threw me away. No pension. No recognition. No apology. Just a desk job and a warning to keep silent.

I kept silent for forty years.

I told no one about my past. Not my mother. Not my friends. Not even my doctor.

But I never stopped paying into the fund. Because the handler told me it would take care of me. And because I had nowhere else to put my hope.

You did not throw me away.

You kept your promise.

You gave me a retirement.

You gave me a chance to die with dignity.

I am grateful. I am more grateful than I can express in words.

If there is anything I can do to repay you, anything at all, I will do it.

I have no money. I have no connections. But I have a story. And sometimes stories are worth more than money.

Thank you for not forgetting me.

Thank you for not throwing me away.

Sincerely,

Tanaka Hiroshi

He read the letter twice.

The first time, he saw only his gratitude.

The second time, he saw his loneliness.

I have no one left.

No one to call.

No one to visit.

No one to hold my hand when the pain becomes too much.

He placed his phone on the bedside table.

The pain in his stomach had subsided to a dull ache. The medication was starting to work. He could sleep now, if he wanted to.

But he did not want to.

I want to remember.

I want to remember who I was before the disease.

I want to remember Onoda Eiji.

I want to remember the years when I was useful.

He closed his eyes.

The memories came, unbidden.

The first meeting. A coffee shop in Ginza. Onoda Eiji sitting across from him, wearing a gray suit and a smile that did not reach his eyes.

"Tanaka-san. I have heard good things about your work at the Ministry of Finance."

"I am just an accountant."

"Accountants see things that others miss. We need someone who sees things."

"What kind of things?"

"Irregularities. Patterns. Names that should not be there."

"I do not understand."

"You will."

He had understood. Within a year, he was providing weekly reports on the banking sector. Names, dates, amounts. Transactions that the Ministry had tried to hide. Accounts that existed only on paper.

He had thought he was helping his country.

He had been helping himself.

The money was good. The work was interesting. The handler was professional.

But the handler was not a friend.

The fund was the only friend I had.

He opened his eyes.

The ceiling was still there. The pain was still there. The loneliness was still there.

I cannot send this letter.

If I send it, the manager will know I was a CIA informant. The fund will be compromised. The other clients will be at risk.

I cannot risk it.

I have to stay silent.

I have to die with my secrets intact.

He deleted the letter.

The screen went blank.

It is better this way.

Better to say nothing.

Better to disappear without a trace.

Better to be forgotten.

He reached into the drawer of his bedside table and pulled out a small photograph.

It was black and white, edges frayed, creased from years of handling. The image showed a young woman in a kimono, standing in front of a temple. She was smiling. She was beautiful.

My mother.

The only person who ever loved me.

The only person I ever let down.

He had visited her grave once, after the arrest. He had stood in the rain, staring at the headstone, saying nothing.

I am sorry, Mother.

I am sorry for the shame I brought you.

I am sorry for the lies I told.

I am sorry for dying alone.

He placed the photograph back in the drawer.

The pain in his stomach began to increase again.

I need more medication.

I need to sleep.

I need to survive.

Just a little longer.

Just until the letters are done.

Just until I have said everything I need to say.

At 5:47 a.m., the sun began to rise.

Tanaka Hiroshi lay in his bed, watching the light seep through the curtains. The pain had become a constant companion, a weight he carried in his chest and his stomach and his bones.

He picked up his phone.

Draft - Unsent Letter

To: The Fund Manager

From: Tanaka Hiroshi, Client ID T9-104-777

Date: February 10, 2025

He began to type again.

Dear Manager,

I do not know if you will ever read this. I do not know if I will ever send it.

But I need to say this, even if no one hears it.

I am dying.

The doctors tell me I have six months. Maybe less. Maybe more. They are not sure. They never are.

I am not afraid of dying. I have been preparing for it my whole life.

But I am afraid of dying with nothing left unsaid.

I have a letter. A real letter. A letter I will send through the fund's channel, like the others. But this one is different.

This one is for you.

I want you to know that you saved me. Not just financially. Not just medically. You saved my hope.

I had given up on the world. I had given up on people. I had given up on myself.

But you showed me that someone cares. Someone who has never seen my face. Someone who has never heard my voice. Someone anonymous, like me.

You are the only person I trust.

You are the only person who has never let me down.

I do not know your name. I do not know your face. But I know your heart.

And that is enough.

Thank you.

Sincerely,

Tanaka Hiroshi

He did not read this letter twice.

He closed the document, turned off his phone, and lay back on the pillow.

I will send it tomorrow.

Or the next day.

When I have the strength.

When the pain is less.

When I am ready to say goodbye.

The sun rose higher.

The apartment filled with light.

And Tanaka Hiroshi, former CIA informant, former Ministry of Finance accountant, current cancer patient, drifted into a sleep filled with dreams of green hills and gray suits and promises that had been kept.

At 8:30 a.m., a phone rang.

He did not answer.

It rang again, then stopped.

He did not wake.

At 9:00 a.m., a text message arrived.

From: Unknown

To: Tanaka Hiroshi

"Your medical file has been flagged. Pinnacle Verification Services is requesting an audit of your withdrawal. Please contact us immediately to confirm your identity. Failure to comply within 72 hours will result in a freeze on your remaining balance."

He did not read it.

He was still dreaming of the handler, of a coffee shop in Ginza, of a promise that had been made and kept.

I am safe.

The fund will protect me.

The fund will always protect me.

He slept on.

The sun continued to rise.

And outside, in the street below, a young woman in a black coat checked her phone and wrote down the address of the apartment building.

Chapter 14: A Certain Name's Confusion

The document arrived at 10:47 a.m. on a Tuesday.

Kinoshita Kenji had been waiting for it for three days. Three days of refreshing his email, checking the mailbox in his apartment building's lobby, and pacing the length of his office while his debt collection cases piled up on his desk.

Three days of wondering if he had made a mistake by continuing the investigation.

He had not told anyone about his meeting with the scarred man named Takahashi. He had not told Elena. He had not told his mother. He had not even told the unknown client who had hired him in the first place.

Some secrets are better kept alone.

The envelope was plain brown, no return address, no postmark. It had been hand-delivered, slipped under his door sometime during the night.

He picked it up. The paper was thick, high-quality, the kind used by government offices and law firms.

This is it.

This is the fund's registration document.

This is the proof I need.

He carried the envelope to his desk, sat down, and opened it with a letter opener that had belonged to his father.

Inside was a single sheet of paper.

And at the bottom of that paper, typed in bold, unmistakable letters, was a name:

YAMADA BOUSHI

Kinoshita stared at the name.

He blinked.

He read it again.

Yamada Boushi.

Yamada Boushi?

He read it a third time. Then a fourth. Then a fifth.

The name did not change.

Yamada Boushi.

山田某氏.

"Yamada Anonymous."

He sat back in his chair.

The sound of his own breathing filled the room.

This is a joke.

This has to be a joke.

No one is actually named Yamada Boushi.

He picked up the phone and called the only contact he had at the Ministry of Justice—a bored clerk named Suzuki who had helped him with a background check once, four years ago.

"Suzuki-san. It's Kinoshita. I need a favor."

"I'm busy."

"I need you to run a name through the corporate registry."

"The fee is ¥5,000."

"I'll pay it."

"Name?"

"Yamada Boushi."

There was a long silence on the other end of the line.

Then a low, suspicious voice: "Are you messing with me?"

"No. I need a corporate record for Yamada Boushi. Manager of a fund called 株式会社某社."

Another silence.

"Kinoshita-san... that's a redaction. It's a placeholder. It means 'Anonymous.'"

"I know what it means."

"Then why are you asking me to run it?"

"Because I need to know if it's real."

"It's not real. It's a fake name. Someone is playing games with you."

"Just run it. Please."

Suzuki sighed—a long, theatrical sound that carried forty years of bureaucratic frustration.

"Fine. I'll run it. But I'm charging you double."

"The fee is ¥5,000."

"Double. For the humiliation."

Kinoshita hung up and stared at the document again.

Yamada Boushi.

Who names their child "Anonymous"?

Who registers a company with a fake name and expects it to stand up to scrutiny?

Who is this person?

He leaned forward and examined the document more closely. The registration was dated 2012. The address was a PO box in Shinjuku. The capital was ¥10 million. The purpose was listed as "Asset Management."

Everything about the document was perfectly normal.

Except for the name.

Yamada Boushi.

It's a redaction.

It's a deliberate, intentional, mocking redaction.

And I have to find out what's behind it.

His phone buzzed.

Text Message

From: Suzuki

"I ran it. It's registered. Name: Yamada Boushi. DOB: November 15, 1991. Address: Shinjuku Business Center, PO Box 204. Status: Active."

Kinoshita read the message twice.

November 15, 1991.

That would make her thirty-four years old now.

Thirty-four and running a ¥2.8 trillion pension fund.

Thirty-four and hiding behind a name that means "Anonymous."

Thirty-four and laughing at the world.

He did not laugh.

He was too angry to laugh.

This is a test.

This is a test of my competence.

The fund's manager is playing games.

She named herself "Yamada Anonymous" and dared me to find her real identity.

And so far, I am losing.

He called his contact on the dark web—a hacker who used the name "Kitsune" and operated out of a coffee shop in Akihabara. The hacker had helped him before, digging up records that the government had tried to bury.

"I need you to unredact a name."

"Unredact is not a word."

"Uncover. Reveal. Find out what's hidden behind the redaction."

"Send me the document."

Kinoshita scanned the registration document and sent it to Kitsune's encrypted email address.

"I'll pay the usual rate."

"¥50,000."

"Fine."

"I'll call you when I find something."

He waited.

One hour.

Two hours.

Three hours.

At 2:17 p.m., his phone rang.

Kitsune's voice was flat, professional, utterly devoid of emotion.

"There is nothing behind it."

"What do you mean, nothing?"

"The name is not redacted. There is no hidden text. No metadata. No encryption. The document you sent is the complete record."

"That's impossible."

"It's not impossible. It's just improbable. The registration document for 株式会社某社 lists the manager as Yamada Boushi. There are no edits, no layers, no obfuscation techniques. The record is exactly as it appears."

"Then she's using a fake name."

"Maybe. But the registration is real. The tax ID is real. The address is a PO box, but the PO box is registered to that name. If it's a fake identity, it's been maintained for thirteen years."

Kinoshita's head began to ache.

Thirteen years.

Thirteen years of maintaining a fake identity.

Thirteen years of paying taxes under a name that means "Anonymous."

Thirteen years of never getting caught.

"Kitsune... run the name through every database you have. Criminal records. Civil records. Birth records. Death records. Anything. I need to know if Yamada Boushi is a real person."

"I already did."

"And?"

"It's inconclusive."

"What does that mean?"

"There is a birth record for a Yamada Boushi. Date of birth: November 15, 1991. Place of birth: Osaka Prefecture. Mother: Yamada Honnin. Father: Deceased. No siblings. No known living relatives."

Kinoshita's blood ran cold.

There is a birth record.

A real birth record.

For a name that sounds like a joke.

"Kitsune... are you telling me that someone actually named their child Yamada Boushi?"

"I am telling you that the Japanese birth registry shows a person named Yamada Boushi. Whether that person is the manager of the fund is a separate question."

"Can you find a photograph? A school record? Anything that confirms identity?"

"I can try. But it will take time. And money."

"How much money?"

"Another ¥100,000. Upfront."

Kinoshita closed his eyes.

¥100,000.

That's half my monthly salary.

That's my mother's rent for three months.

That's a gamble I cannot afford to lose.

"Do it."

He hung up and stared at the registration document.

Yamada Boushi.

November 15, 1991.

Osaka Prefecture.

Mother: Yamada Honnin.

Father: Deceased.

No siblings.

No known living relatives.

Who are you?

Who names their child "Anonymous"?

And what kind of woman grows up with that name and decides to run a ¥2.8 trillion pension fund?

He picked up his phone and called the only person he could think of who might know the answer.

Elena picked up on the third ring.

"Kinoshita-san. I told you not to call me again."

"I found the registration document."

There was a pause.

"And?"

"The manager's name is Yamada Boushi."

"That is not a real name."

"It's on the registration. It's on the tax ID. It's even in the birth registry."

"Is that a joke?"

"I wish it was. But Kitsune confirmed it. The name is real. The registration is real. The manager's name is literally 'Anonymous.'"

Elena was silent for a long moment.

Then she laughed.

It was not a mocking laugh. It was a laugh of pure, unadulterated amazement.

"Kinoshita-san... do you understand what this means?"

"It means I wasted ¥100,000 on a name that sounds like a redaction."

"No. It means you are dealing with someone who has been hiding in plain sight since birth. She was given a name that no one takes seriously. She used that name to register a fund that manages trillions of yen. She is not hiding. She is playing a game that no one else understands."

"Then how do I find her real identity?"

"You don't. She has been running this operation for thirteen years. If she didn't want you to know her real name, you would never find it."

"But I have the birth record."

"The birth record is for a child born in 1991. That child is now thirty-four years old. Do you know what she looks like? Do you know where she lives? Do you know anything about her?"

"Not yet."

"Then you have nothing."

Kinoshita felt the weight of her words.

She is right.

I have a name that sounds like a joke.

I have a registration that leads nowhere.

I have a PO box in Shinjuku.

I have nothing.

"Kinoshita-san."

"What?"

"Walk away. Before it's too late."

"I can't. My mother is in danger."

"Then find another way to protect her."

"There is no other way."

He hung up.

The room was silent.

He stared at the registration document, at the name that had consumed his life for the past two weeks.

Yamada Boushi.

A name that means "Anonymous."

A name that is not a redaction.

A name that is real.

He began to laugh.

It was not a happy laugh. It was a laugh of frustration, of irony, of a man who had been outsmarted by a woman whose name made no sense.

She won.

She won without ever meeting me.

She won by existing.

She won by being born with a name that no one believes.

And I am still chasing shadows.

He picked up the registration document and placed it in his desk drawer.

I will find you, Yamada Boushi.

I will find your real name.

I will find your real face.

I will find the person behind the joke.

And when I do, I will demand to know why you chose this life.

I will demand to know why you play games with people's lives.

I will demand to know what kind of woman names herself "Anonymous" and dares the world to take her seriously.

He locked the drawer.

He stood up.

He walked to the window.

The streets of Ikebukuro were crowded with people who had no idea that a woman named "Yamada Anonymous" was running a pension fund worth ¥2.8 trillion.

They are living their lives.

They are paying their bills.

They are getting old.

And they will never know that someone is taking care of them.

Someone who named herself after a joke.

Someone who has been laughing at the world—and winning—for thirteen years.

His phone buzzed again.

Text Message

From: Unknown

"You found the name. Now find the server. 11 days left."

He stared at the message.

The server.

The source of the fund's power.

The code behind the pension.

The engine that drives the trillions.

If I find the server, I find the truth.

If I find the truth, I find the manager.

If I find the manager, I save my mother.

He typed a reply:

"I'm working on it."

He did not tell the client that he had hit a dead end.

He did not tell the client that the manager's name was Yamada Boushi.

He did not tell the client that the joke was on him.

Some secrets are better kept alone.

He closed his phone.

The sun was beginning to set.

The world was turning orange.

And Kinoshita Kenji, debt collector by trade, investigator by necessity, was standing in his office, staring at a name that meant nothing and everything at the same time.

Yamada Boushi.

Yamada Anonymous.

Whoever you are, wherever you are...

I will find you.

I will find the truth.

And I will make sure my mother is safe.

That is my promise.

That is my debt.

And I always collect my debts.

He pulled out a piece of paper and a pen.

He wrote the name in kanji:

山田某氏

Yamada Boushi.

The name that is not a redaction.

The name that is the joke.

The name that is the truth.

He folded the paper and placed it in his pocket.

I will find you.

I will.

At 7:32 p.m., he left his office.

He walked to the station.

He took the train to Shinjuku.

He stood in front of the Business Center, staring at the PO box that was listed as the manager's address.

The building was ordinary. The lobby was ordinary. The mail slots were ordinary.

And inside one of those mail slots, behind the address of PO Box 204, was the life of a woman who had spent thirteen years hiding in plain sight.

I could break the lock.

I could open the box.

I could find the letters, the bills, the traces of her existence.

But I won't.

Because that is not how you find someone like her.

You find someone like her by understanding the joke.

You find someone like her by laughing with her.

You find someone like her by realizing that the joke is not on you.

The joke is on everyone who thought they could find her.

And she is still laughing.

He turned away from the Business Center.

He walked back to the station.

He took the train home.

And he spent the rest of the night staring at the name he had written on the piece of paper.

Yamada Boushi.

Yamada Boushi.

Yamada Boushi.

The name repeated in his mind like a mantra.

It is real.

It is a person.

And I am going to find her.

I am going to find the woman who named herself "Anonymous."

I am going to find the woman who laughed at the world.

I am going to find the woman who built a pension fund on a joke.

And when I do...

I am going to ask her one question.

"Why?"

Chapter 15: A Certain Overdue Coffee

The clock on Yamada's computer screen read 6:47 p.m.

She stared at it for a full ten seconds, waiting for the number to change, as if time itself might be playing a trick on her.

6:47.

I am leaving at 6:47.

I am leaving before my supervisor can find me.

I am leaving before anyone can ask me to stay late.

She saved her spreadsheet, closed the browser window, and stood up from her desk with the quiet efficiency of a woman who had spent nine years perfecting the art of unnoticed departure.

Endō Yuka looked up from her computer. "Leaving early, Yamada-san?"

Early.

It is 6:47 p.m.

I have been here since 8:30 a.m.

I am leaving at exactly the time I am supposed to leave.

But she said none of this. She simply smiled the smile she had practiced until it felt natural—a small, apologetic curve of the lips that said I know I am being unreasonable, please forgive me.

"Meeting an old friend. High school."

Endō nodded, her expression unreadable. "Have fun."

The word fun hung in the air between them, a foreign concept in an office where overtime was expected and weekends were for catching up on sleep.

Fun.

I remember fun.

I think.

She walked to the station at her usual pace—not too fast, not too slow. The kind of pace that suggested a woman with nowhere urgent to be and nothing important to hide.

The train was crowded. She found a spot near the door and held on to the overhead strap, her body swaying with the motion of the carriage.

This is normal.

This is what normal people do.

They take crowded trains home.

They meet friends for coffee.

They complain about rent.

She closed her eyes and let the rhythm of the train carry her.

The café was in Shimokitazawa, a narrow space tucked between a second-hand record store and a vintage clothing shop. The lighting was warm, the music was jazz, and the smell of freshly ground coffee filled the air like a promise.

Her friend was already there.

Yoshida Aiko—Aiko to her friends, Yoshida-san to everyone else—sat at a table by the window, nursing a latte and scrolling through her phone. She looked up as Yamada approached, and her face broke into a smile that was genuine and uncomplicated.

"Boushi-chan! You're early!"

Boushi-chan.

The nickname that stuck.

Aiko had called her that since high school, back when everyone thought her name was a typo on the class roster.

"I'm always early."

"I know. That's what makes you you."

Yamada sat down across from her friend. The chair was wooden and slightly wobbly. The table was small and covered in rings from previous coffee cups.

This is normal.

This is friendship.

This is what I am fighting for.

They ordered.

Coffee for Yamada. Another latte for Aiko. A slice of cheesecake to share.

"So," Aiko said, leaning forward, "how's work?"

How is work.

I spend my days reconciling accounts for a small tax accounting firm.

I spend my nights managing a pension fund worth ¥2.8 trillion.

I have an LLM that I pay ¥10,000 a month to maintain.

I have clients who would be assassinated if their names appeared on any official register.

I have a yakuza who has seen my face and knows my address.

And I have a stalker who is currently investigating me through a shell company in Hong Kong.

That is how work is.

"Same as always," she said. "Spreadsheets and tax forms."

Aiko laughed. "You always say that. I don't believe you."

"It's true."

"You have the most boring job in the world."

"That's why I like it."

Lies.

All lies.

But these lies are the walls that protect the truth.

And the truth would frighten her.

The cheesecake arrived.

They ate in comfortable silence for a few minutes, the way old friends do.

Then Aiko sighed.

"I have to move."

Yamada looked up. "What?"

"My rent is going up next month. ¥15,000 more. I can't afford it."

"How much is it now?"

"¥120,000. For a one-bedroom in Suginami."

¥120,000.

My apartment is ¥65,000.

But I could pay ¥120,000 without noticing.

I could pay a year of ¥120,000 right now, from the cash I keep in my desk drawer.

I could buy the building she lives in.

I could buy the whole street.

But I can't say that.

I can't even hint at it.

"I'm sorry," Yamada said. "That's rough."

Aiko shrugged. "It's Tokyo. Everyone's rent is going up. I'm thinking about moving to Saitama."

"Saitama?"

"Cheaper. More space. Longer commute."

"You would hate that commute."

"I know. But I can't afford to stay here. Unless..."

Unless what?

Unless I offer to help.

Unless I offer to pay the difference.

Unless I reveal that I am sitting on trillions of yen and she is struggling to pay ¥15,000 extra per month.

I can't.

I can't even think about it.

If I help her, she will ask questions.

If she asks questions, I will have to lie.

If I lie, she will eventually find out the truth.

And if she finds out the truth, she will be in danger.

Yamada took a sip of her coffee.

Aiko does not know about the danger.

Aiko does not know about the yakuza.

Aiko does not know about the shell company.

Aiko does not know about the client who is trying to find the fund's server.

And if I help her, I will drag her into that world.

I cannot drag her into that world.

She has a life.

A normal life.

A life that does not involve trillions of yen or death threats or shell companies in Hong Kong.

"I'm sorry," Yamada said again. "I wish I could help."

Aiko smiled, but it was a tired smile. "You don't have to help. You're in the same boat as me."

No.

I am not.

I am in a completely different boat.

A boat made of ¥2.8 trillion.

A boat that I am trying very hard to hide from everyone I love.

Especially from you.

They finished the cheesecake.

They ordered more coffee.

They talked about other things.

Aiko's job at a real estate agency. Her mother's arthritis. The cat she had adopted last year, a gray tabby named Miso.

"Miso is getting fat," Aiko said. "I think I'm feeding her too much."

"How much are you feeding her?"

"Two cans a day."

"That's too much."

"I know. But she looks at me with those big eyes and I can't say no."

Two cans of cat food.

¥200 per can.

¥400 per day.

¥12,000 per month.

More than she can afford, probably.

But she does it anyway because the cat makes her happy.

Because the cat is worth it.

Because she has nothing else.

Not like me.

I have everything.

And I can tell no one.

At 9:15 p.m., the waitress brought the bill.

Aiko reached for it. "Let me get this. You came all the way here."

"No," Yamada said. "I'll pay."

"Are you sure?"

"I'm sure."

Yamada took the bill. ¥3,200. Two coffees and a slice of cheesecake.

She reached into her purse and pulled out her wallet.

¥500 bills.

¥1,000 bills.

Enough for coffee.

Not enough to raise suspicion.

Not enough to make her wonder why I have ¥2.8 trillion in a pension fund and no visible sign of wealth.

She counted out the money and placed it on the table.

"There. Done."

Aiko smiled. "Thank you. Next time is on me."

"Next time."

If there is a next time.

If I survive the audit.

If the yakuza don't find me first.

If the client doesn't discover the server.

If the world doesn't collapse.

Next time.

They walked out of the café together.

The night air was cold. The streets were crowded with people heading home from dinner, from drinks, from lives that were simpler than hers.

"I'll walk you to the station," Aiko said.

"You don't have to."

"I want to."

They walked in silence.

Shimokitazawa at night was alive with music spilling out of bars, with laughter from groups of friends, with the hum of a thousand conversations.

This is what I am protecting.

Not just my clients.

Not just the fund.

This.

The possibility of a normal life.

The possibility of meeting a friend for coffee.

The possibility of walking through a crowded street and feeling like I belong.

Even if I don't.

Even if I never will.

At the station entrance, Aiko stopped.

"Boushi-chan."

"Yes?"

"Are you okay?"

No.

I am not okay.

I am managing a fund under multiple threats.

I am being investigated by a man who knows my name.

I am being watched by a woman in a black coat.

I am exhausted.

I am terrified.

I am alone.

"I'm fine."

"You don't seem fine."

She can see it.

She has always been able to see it.

That is why I love her.

That is why I cannot tell her the truth.

"I'm just tired. Work has been busy."

Aiko looked at her for a long moment.

Then she reached out and squeezed her hand.

"Take care of yourself, Boushi-chan."

"I will."

"And if you ever need anything... you can call me. I don't care what time it is."

I need you to not ask questions.

I need you to stay away from me.

I need you to be safe.

And the only way to keep you safe is to keep you at arm's length.

"Thank you, Aiko."

"See you next month?"

"See you next month."

I hope.

Yamada watched her friend disappear into the station.

Then she turned and walked back toward her own train.

The streets were still crowded, but they felt empty now.

I almost told her.

I almost said, "I can help with your rent."

I almost said, "I have more money than you can imagine."

I almost said, "Let me take care of you."

But I didn't.

I couldn't.

Because if I start helping one person, I will have to help everyone.

And if I help everyone, I will have nothing left.

Not even the lie.

Not even the hiding.

Not even the loneliness.

She took the train to Higashi-Koganei.

She walked the fifteen minutes to her apartment building.

She climbed the stairs to the fourth floor.

She unlocked the door.

The apartment was dark. Empty. Quiet.

She did not turn on the light.

She walked to her desk, sat down, and opened her laptop.

Work never ends.

Even when I have a rare evening off.

Even when I meet an old friend.

Work never ends.

She opened the fund's dashboard.

Balance: ¥2,817,332,400,000.

Daily change: +¥43,200,000.

New clients: 0.

Withdrawals: 0.

Audit flags: 0.

Everything is normal.

Everything is fine.

Except...

She scrolled down to the access logs.

System Activity - Last 24 Hours:

  • 14:32: Internal compliance check (routine)
  • 16:01: Balance inquiry (client portal)
  • 17:44: Withdrawal request (rejected - incomplete documentation)
  • 18:23: LLM update (automated)
  • 19:07: Server backup (automated)
  • 20:15: Public filings accessed (external IP)
  • 20:16: Public filings accessed (external IP)
  • 20:16: Public filings accessed (external IP)
  • 20:17: Public filings accessed (external IP)
  • 20:17: Public filings accessed (external IP)
  • 20:18: Public filings accessed (external IP)

She stopped scrolling.

Public filings accessed.

Seven times in three minutes.

From an external IP.

She clicked on the IP address.

203.0.113.45

Registered to: Blackrock Financial Management, New York, NY.

Blackrock.

The largest asset manager in the world.

Why is Blackrock accessing my public filings?

She opened the public filings page.

It was a simple document, required by Japanese law, listing the fund's basic information: name, registration number, address, manager's name, assets under management, fee structure.

Nothing confidential.

Nothing that would reveal the fund's holdings or strategy.

But still...

Why would Blackrock care about a small Japanese pension fund?

Why would they access the filing seven times in three minutes?

Who at Blackrock is doing this?

She checked the IP history.

The same IP had accessed the filing 47 times in the past month.

The first access was on January 15.

The most recent access was today.

47 times.

Someone at Blackrock is very interested in this fund.

Someone at Blackrock is watching.

She felt a chill run down her spine.

This is not a coincidence.

This is not a routine check.

This is someone looking for something.

But what?

She opened a search window and typed:

Blackrock Japanese pension fund interest

The results were unremarkable. A few articles about Blackrock's expansion into Japanese markets. A press release about their Tokyo office. Nothing specific.

She tried another search:

Blackrock audit request Japan

Nothing useful.

She tried:

Pinnacle Verification Services Blackrock

No results.

Maybe it is a coincidence.

Maybe someone at Blackrock is doing routine research.

Maybe I am paranoid.

But being paranoid has kept me alive for thirteen years.

And being paranoid is not going to stop now.

She minimized the search window and opened her trading platform.

The Vietnamese small-cap market had been performing well.

She had bought ¥200 million worth of stocks two weeks ago.

The value had increased by ¥300 million.

A good return.

A very good return.

She looked at her watchlist.

Vietnam Engine & Agricultural Machinery Corporation.

Binh Minh Plastics.

FPT Corporation.

Mobile World Group.

Vinh Hoan Corporation.

All stable.

All growing.

All undervalued.

She bought another ¥50 million.

If Blackrock is watching, let them watch.

Let them see a fund that makes smart investments and never panics.

Let them see a fund that is boring and predictable and safe.

Let them see nothing unusual.

Because there is nothing unusual here.

Just a woman managing a pension fund from her apartment in Higashi-Koganei.

Just a woman who never sleeps.

Just a woman who will never stop.

She finished the trade.

She closed the platform.

She checked the access logs again.

No new activity.

Maybe they are done for the night.

Maybe I am safe.

For now.

She closed her laptop.

The apartment was still dark.

The clock on the wall read 11:47 p.m.

I should sleep.

I should rest.

I should take care of myself.

But I can't.

Because if I sleep, I will dream.

And if I dream, I will see her.

My mother.

Eating spoiled onigiri.

Dying in a hospital bed because she had no pension.

Because there was no one to take care of her.

Because the world does not care about people like her.

But I care.

I care more than anyone knows.

I care more than I can express.

I care with every trade I make.

With every risk I take.

With every secret I keep.

I care.

And that is why I cannot sleep.

She stood up and walked to the window.

The street below was empty.

The apartment building across the way was dark.

Somewhere out there, someone is trying to find me.

Someone at Blackrock.

Someone at Pinnacle.

Someone named Kinoshita Kenji.

They are all searching.

They are all looking.

They are all trying to uncover the truth.

But the truth is simple.

The truth is this:

I am a woman who saw her mother die because the world did not care.

And I decided to care.

Even if it costs me everything.

Even if it costs me my life.

Even if it costs me my sanity.

I decided to care.

And I will not stop.

Not for Blackrock.

Not for the yakuza.

Not for anyone.

She turned away from the window.

She walked back to her desk.

She opened her laptop again.

The dashboard glowed in the dark.

You have 3 unread messages.

She opened the first one.

From: Client ID N5-209-883 (Tanaka Yūsuke)

Subject: Thank you

She opened the second one.

From: Client ID T9-104-777 (Tanaka Hiroshi)

Subject: My mother's photograph

She opened the third one.

From: System

Subject: Security Notice

She read the security notice.

External scanning detected from IP 203.0.113.45

Frequency: 47 accesses in 30 days

Recommendation: Monitor for increased activity

No action required at this time.

She closed the message.

No action required.

For now.

But tomorrow is a new day.

And the scan logs will grow.

And I will still be here.

Watching.

Waiting.

Preparing.

She opened the first client message.

Dear Manager,

I received the medical withdrawal. The money is in my account. The chemotherapy starts next week.

I am writing to thank you. I have no other words. Just thank you.

You saved my life.

Sincerely,

Tanaka Yūsuke

She saved the message.

She would reply tomorrow.

She would thank him for his trust.

She would promise to keep him safe.

These are the messages that keep me going.

These are the reasons I do what I do.

These are the people I have saved.

And these are the people I will continue to save.

As long as I can.

She turned off her laptop.

She lay down on her futon.

She closed her eyes.

Tomorrow, I will meet Aiko again.

Tomorrow, I will pretend to be normal.

Tomorrow, I will go to work and sit at my desk and reconcile accounts.

And tomorrow night, I will come home and check the scan logs and make more trades.

Because that is my life.

That is my mission.

That is my promise.

And I will never break it.

Not for anything.

Not for anyone.

She fell asleep with the image of her mother's face in her mind.

I will not let them take this from me.

I will not let them take this from you.

I will protect what I have built.

Even if it means protecting it alone.

Even if it means protecting it forever.

Even if it means protecting it until I die.

I will protect it.

Because that is who I am.

That is what I do.

That is why I exist.

Yamada Boushi.

The woman who named herself "Anonymous."

The woman who built a pension fund on a promise.

The woman who will never stop.

Outside, in the street below, the young woman in the black coat checked her phone.

She had been watching the apartment building for three hours.

She had seen the light go on.

She had seen the light go off.

She had seen nothing unusual.

She is home.

She is alone.

She is not expecting visitors.

She typed a message into her phone.

Subject: Status Update

To: Client

Target is at residence. No unusual activity observed. Recommend continued surveillance. Will report again at 0600 hours.

She pocketed the phone.

She adjusted her position.

She settled in for the night.

Another long shift.

Another night of watching.

Another day closer to finding the truth.

The truth about the woman who named herself "Anonymous."

The truth about the fund.

The truth about everything.

She will make a mistake eventually.

They always do.

She waited.

The night grew darker.

The city grew quieter.

And somewhere in the dark, a woman named Yamada Boushi dreamed of her mother, of coffee with an old friend, of the day when everyone would be safe and she could finally rest.

But that day was not today.

And that day would not be tomorrow.

And that day might never come.

But she would keep fighting anyway.

Because that was what she did.

Because that was who she was.

Because she was Yamada Boushi.

And she never stopped.

Chapter 16: A Certain Inquiry

Sarah Chen arrived at the SEC's New York Regional Office at 6:47 a.m.

She had been awake since 4:30, and she had been thinking about the case since 4:32.

The fund is called 株式会社某社. That translates to "A Certain Company, Incorporated." The manager is listed as Yamada Boushi. That translates to "Yamada Anonymous." The address is a P.O. box in Shinjuku. The fund holds approximately ¥2.8 trillion in assets, which is roughly $19 billion at current exchange rates. It trades U.S. securities without registration. It has no KYC procedures. It is operating in clear violation of the Securities Exchange Act of 1934.

And I am going to shut it down.

She swiped her badge at the security checkpoint.

The guard nodded. "Morning, Chen."

"Morning, Frank."

She walked through the lobby, past the elevators, past the coffee cart where a bleary-eyed associate was ordering an espresso with the desperation of a man who had not slept in 36 hours.

That will not be me.

I sleep.

I eat.

I exercise.

I am disciplined.

Discipline is what keeps me from making mistakes.

Mistakes are what happen when you let emotion cloud your judgment.

And my judgment is never clouded.

She took the elevator to the 14th floor.

The Enforcement Division was already humming with activity. Lights were on. Phones were ringing. Lawyers in suits were walking with purpose, carrying files that contained the life stories of people who had made the mistake of thinking they could outsmart the SEC.

Sarah loved this floor.

She loved the energy. The urgency. The feeling that every case mattered, that every violation was a wound that needed to be healed.

The market is a living thing.

And when someone breaks the rules, they poison it.

My job is to remove the poison.

She walked to her cubicle, dropped her bag, and pulled out the file.

株式会社某社.

"A Certain Company."

A name so deliberately vague that it practically screamed "Look at me."

A manager named "Yamada Anonymous."

A fund that was clearly designed to hide its true purpose.

And yet...

And yet, the returns were remarkable.

She had pulled up the performance data last night. A 12.4% annualized return over the past decade. Consistently beating the market. No dramatic losses. No suspicious spikes. Just steady, disciplined growth.

Like someone who knew exactly what they were doing.

Like someone who was playing a long game.

But a long game against whom?

She opened her laptop and began typing.

The presentation had to be perfect. She would be presenting to the Deputy Director of Enforcement at 9:00 a.m. She had fifteen minutes. She would use every second.

Slide one: Overview of the fund.

Slide two: Violations of the Securities Exchange Act.

Slide three: Lack of registration.

Slide four: Absence of KYC procedures.

Slide five: The manager's identity—or lack thereof.

Slide six: Recommended next steps.

She worked quickly, efficiently, her fingers moving across the keyboard with the precision of a concert pianist.

I have been preparing for this case my entire career.

Not this specific case, but this type of case.

The kind where someone thinks they are smarter than the rules.

The kind where someone thinks they can hide behind a name that is not a name.

The kind where someone thinks they will never get caught.

They are wrong.

They are always wrong.

And I am going to prove it.

At 8:55 a.m., she printed the slides, grabbed her binder, and walked to the conference room.

The Deputy Director, a man named Harrison Cole, was already there. He was in his late fifties, with silver hair and the kind of face that had been weathered by decades of reading fraud cases.

He looked up as she entered. "Chen. You're early."

"I'm always early, sir."

"So I've noticed." He gestured to the chair across from him. "Sit down. Tell me about this Japanese fund."

She sat.

She opened her binder.

She began.

"株式会社某社 is a mutual fund registered in Japan. It was established in 2012. Its stated purpose is 'asset management for the benefit of retired persons.' Its manager is listed as Yamada Boushi."

Cole raised an eyebrow. "Yamada Boushi."

"Yes, sir."

"That's not a real name."

"That's what we assumed. But our investigation has confirmed that the name appears on the Japanese corporate registry. It appears on the tax ID. It appears on the birth registry."

"You're telling me someone is actually named 'Yamada Anonymous'?"

"That's what the records indicate."

Cole leaned back in his chair. He did not look amused.

He looks intrigued.

That is good.

Intrigue means he will pay attention.

Intrigue means he will remember this case.

Intrigue means he will give me the resources I need.

"Continue."

She did.

"The fund holds approximately ¥2.8 trillion in assets, which is roughly $19 billion. It trades U.S. securities without registration. It has no KYC procedures. We have evidence that it has been operating for thirteen years without any oversight."

"Any redemptions?"

"None that we can trace. The fund appears to be a closed-end vehicle. New clients are accepted sporadically. Withdrawals are rare."

"So it's a pension fund."

"That's what it claims to be."

"But you don't believe it."

She paused.

This is the question.

This is the question that will determine whether I get the case or whether they hand it off to someone else.

This is the question that will determine whether I can prove myself.

"I believe it is a pension fund," she said carefully. "But I also believe it is operating in a way that violates U.S. securities law. Whether the manager intended to violate the law or simply ignored it is irrelevant. The violation exists. And I am recommending that we send a formal inquiry."

Cole studied her for a long moment.

He is reading me.

He is trying to decide if I am zealous or reckless.

Zealous is good.

Reckless is dangerous.

I have to make sure I sound zealous.

"Send the inquiry," he said. "But proceed carefully. This fund has diplomatic implications. The Japanese government is not going to appreciate an American regulator poking into one of their pension funds."

"I understand."

"Do you?"

"Yes, sir."

I understand that rules exist for a reason.

I understand that the fund is breaking them.

I understand that my job is to enforce them.

And I will.

No matter what.

The inquiry was drafted by 11:00 a.m.

Sarah wrote it herself, using the standard template but adding a few paragraphs that she felt were necessary to convey the seriousness of the situation.

To the Manager of 株式会社某社:

The Securities and Exchange Commission is conducting an inquiry into the activities of your fund. Specifically, we have identified the following potential violations of the Securities Exchange Act of 1934:

1. Trading in U.S. securities without registration.

2. Failure to maintain adequate Know Your Customer procedures.

3. Operating with a manager whose identity cannot be verified.

Please provide the following information within 30 days:

[List of requested documents]

Failure to respond may result in further enforcement action, including but not limited to the freezing of assets and the imposition of fines.

She read the letter three times.

It is perfect.

It is firm but professional.

It leaves no room for ambiguity.

It is exactly what a letter from the SEC should be.

She signed it.

She addressed it to the P.O. box in Shinjuku.

She handed it to her assistant, a young man named David who had been with the division for six months.

"Send this overnight. Tracked delivery."

"Got it."

"Also, send a copy by fax."

"To the same number?"

"The same number."

David looked at the address. "Yamada Boushi. P.O. Box 204, Shinjuku Business Center. That's... that's not a real name, is it?"

"It's the name on the registration."

"But it says 'Anonymous.'"

"I know what it says."

David opened his mouth to say something else, then closed it.

He is learning.

He is learning that in this job, you do not question the names on the documents.

You question the people behind the names.

And you do it by following the rules.

Because the rules are all we have.

At 2:00 p.m., Sarah gathered her team.

There were four of them: Sarah (lead), David (assistant), a data analyst named Priya, and a junior lawyer named Marcus.

They were seated in a small conference room, a whiteboard covered in notes about the fund.

"I want to know everything about this manager," Sarah said. "Yamada Boushi. I want to know where she was born. I want to know where she went to school. I want to know what she eats for breakfast. I want to know everything."

Priya raised her hand. "The name is a redaction."

"That's what we assumed. But the Japanese corporate registry lists it as a real name. There's a birth record. There's a tax ID. It's all real."

"Then it's an alias."

"Maybe. But it's been maintained for thirteen years. That's not a casual alias. That's a deliberate choice."

Marcus frowned. "What kind of person names themselves 'Anonymous'?"

"Someone who doesn't want to be found."

"Then why leave a trail?"

"Because she knew we would look. And she wanted us to find the name."

Priya leaned forward. "You think it's a game?"

"I think it's a test." Sarah looked at each of them in turn. "I think she wants to see if we can look past the name and find the person. And I think she's confident we won't be able to."

"Can we?"

"We're going to try."

They tried.

For the next three hours, they tried.

Priya ran the name through every database she could access. The FBI's criminal records. Interpol's watchlists. The CIA's open-source intelligence reports. The Department of Treasury's sanctions lists.

Yamada Boushi.

No matches.

No criminal record.

No sanctions.

No watchlist.

Nothing.

Marcus tried to find the name in social media. Facebook, Twitter, Instagram, LinkedIn, even obscure forums and message boards.

Yamada Boushi.

No profiles.

No posts.

No comments.

No mentions.

Nothing.

David tried to unredact the name.

He opened the corporate registration in a document editor. He zoomed in on the name. He tried to see if there was hidden text under the bold letters.

Nothing.

He tried changing the font. He tried adjusting the color. He tried running it through a OCR program that claimed to detect hidden layers.

Nothing.

"Chen," he said, "I can't unredact it. It's not a redaction. It's the actual text. The name is literally 'Yamada Boushi.'"

"Keep trying."

"I've tried everything."

"Then try something else."

David sighed and turned back to his computer.

He is frustrated.

He is frustrated because he cannot solve the puzzle.

He is frustrated because the name is a joke and he cannot figure out the punchline.

But I am not frustrated.

I am focused.

Because if the name is not a redaction, then it is a choice.

And choices reveal character.

And character reveals intent.

And intent reveals motive.

And motive is what I need to find.

At 5:47 p.m., Sarah called a break.

"Go home," she said. "Rest. We'll resume tomorrow."

"What about the inquiry?" David asked.

"It's been sent. We'll wait for a response."

"And if they don't respond?"

"Then we escalate."

Escalation.

Freezing the accounts.

Seizing the assets.

Making a public example of the fund.

That is the final step.

But I am not there yet.

First, I need to understand the person behind the name.

And the only way to understand her is to follow the rules.

Because the rules are the only thing that will lead me to the truth.

They packed up their things and left.

Sarah stayed.

She sat alone in the conference room, staring at the whiteboard.

Yamada Boushi.

Age 34.

Born in Osaka.

Manager of a ¥2.8 trillion pension fund.

No criminal record.

No social media presence.

No visible life.

Who are you?

What made you choose this path?

What made you decide to spend your life hiding in plain sight?

She thought about her own path.

She had grown up in a small town in Ohio. Her father was a lawyer. Her mother was a nurse. They had taught her that rules existed for a reason.

Follow the rules, and you will be safe.

Follow the rules, and you will be successful.

Follow the rules, and you will never have to worry.

She had believed them.

She had followed the rules.

She had gone to law school. She had joined the SEC. She had worked her way up through the ranks, always following the rules, always doing what was expected of her.

And then, seven years ago, she had made a mistake.

It was a small case. A charity-run investment pool in rural Florida. The manager was a retired schoolteacher named Margaret O'Connell. She had been raising money for a children's hospital, investing it in a simple portfolio of bonds, and returning the profits to the charity.

Sarah had been the junior lawyer on the case. She had reviewed the file. The pool was operating without proper registration. The disclosures were incomplete. Technically, it was a violation.

But Margaret O'Connell was a sweet woman. She wore floral dresses and spoke softly. She had been a teacher for forty years. She had no criminal record. She was clearly trying to do good.

She is not a criminal.

She is a good person who made a mistake.

The rules exist to catch criminals, not to punish good people.

Sarah had recommended leniency. A warning letter. A small fine. No further action.

Her supervisor had agreed.

Let it slide, Chen. She's not hurting anyone.

Yes, sir.

Thank you, sir.

And then, two years later, the Department of Justice had raided that same charity.

The pool had been a front. Margaret O'Connell was not a retired schoolteacher. She was the sister of a mid-level cartel lieutenant. The "charity" donations were drug money being laundered through a shell structure that looked like a bond portfolio. The children's hospital never received a cent.

The agents found ledgers. They found wire transfers to Colombia. They found photographs of Margaret O'Connell meeting with known traffickers in a Miami hotel room.

Thirty-seven children died because the hospital could not afford the treatments that the charity was supposed to fund.

Sarah had read the case file in her office, her hands shaking.

I let her slide.

I felt sorry for her.

I thought she was a good person.

And thirty-seven children died.

She still could hear their names if she closed her eyes. She had made herself memorize them.

That is why I never make exceptions.

That is why I never let sympathy cloud my judgment.

That is why I will pursue this fund with everything I have.

Because the moment you bend the rules, someone dies.

And I will not let that happen again.

She looked at the whiteboard again.

Yamada Boushi.

She is hiding something.

She is hiding behind a name that is not a name.

She is running a fund that accepts clients without KYC.

She is trading U.S. securities without registration.

The rules are clear.

The violations are clear.

And I will shut her down.

Not because I want to.

But because I have to.

Because the rules exist for a reason.

And because I will not be responsible for another thirty-seven deaths.

She stood up.

She erased the whiteboard.

She walked out of the conference room.

The office was quiet now. Most of the staff had gone home. The lights were dimmed.

She walked to her cubicle and sat down.

Her computer screen glowed.

She checked her email.

Subject: Tracking Notification

From: FedEx

Your package has been delivered to: Shinjuku Business Center, PO Box 204.

Delivered at: 8:47 p.m. local time.

Signed by: No signature required.

The inquiry has arrived.

The fund has received my letter.

Now we wait.

Wait for a response.

Wait for the manager to make a move.

Wait for the truth to reveal itself.

And when it does, I will be ready.

I will not bend.

I will not break.

I will follow the rules, every single one of them, to the letter.

Because that is the only way I can live with myself.

That is the only way I can make sure no one else dies.

She closed her email.

She opened the fund's public filings.

She looked at the name again.

Yamada Boushi.

Yamada Anonymous.

Who are you?

Are you a criminal?

Are you a saint?

Are you just someone who made the same mistake I did?

It does not matter.

What matters is that you are breaking the rules.

And I am going to stop you.

Because I have to.

Because I know what happens when people like you are allowed to operate unchecked.

I know the cost of sympathy.

I know the price of leniency.

And I will not pay it again.

She packed her bag.

She turned off her computer.

She walked to the elevator.

The building was silent.

The streets outside were dark.

She hailed a cab and gave the driver her address.

Tomorrow, I will continue.

Tomorrow, I will send more inquiries.

Tomorrow, I will dig deeper.

Tomorrow, I will find the truth.

And when I do, I will enforce the rules.

Every single one of them.

No exceptions.

No mercy.

Because that is the only way to keep the market clean.

That is the only way to keep people safe.

That is the only way to make sure no one dies because I felt sorry for someone who did not deserve it.

The cab pulled away from the curb.

The city lights blurred past.

And Sarah Chen, SEC enforcement lawyer, stared out the window and thought about a woman she had never met, who was running a pension fund worth billions of dollars from a P.O. box in Shinjuku.

I will find you, Yamada Boushi.

I will find you because I have to.

Because the rules exist for a reason.

And because I will not let you become the next Margaret O'Connell.

I will not let you destroy lives while I look the other way.

I will follow the rules.

I will enforce the rules.

And I will not make exceptions.

Not for you.

Not for anyone.

Never again.
